Hardware Accelerator for Spiking
Neural Networks

==PAGE:3==
Abstract
The area of artificial intelligence is becoming increasingly popular and has wide-spread
use already. In particular the sub-area of artificial neural networks (ANNs) has seen
much attention and serves as the basis for many modern software products due to their
flexibility and learning capabilities. However, the increased number of such models de-
ployed in mobile low-power devices introduces a need to do such processing efficiently
offline. This is difficult using regular ANNs as their evaluation is most often based on
expensive floating-point operations and many memory accesses. This has led to the intro-
duction of computing-oriented spiking neural networks (SNNs) as a potential alternative.
Basing themselves on binary events in time known as spikes, SNNs remove the need for
costly multiplications, enabling small, efficient, low-power accelerator implementations.
This masters thesis presents the design and implementation of a number of opti-
mizations to an existing SNN accelerator targeting a significant reduction in its power
consumption. Firstly, a network is trained for a keyword spotting application, and next
the network is mapped to the accelerator. After introduction of clock gating and an
alternative memory layout with different quantization, the optimized accelerator was
found to consume fewer FPGA resources and 67.5% less power than the baseline design
when targeting a Xilinx Kintex-7 FPGA on a Digilent Genesys-2 board.

Terminology
The following terminology will be used throughout the report. Basic knowledge of digital
hardware design is expected from the reader.
1. Spiking Neural Network (SNN) terminology
• Spike - Represents a binary event in time, which models an impulse trans-
mitted within a mammal brain.
• Neuron - Represents the main component of SNNs, which are networks, i.e.,
graphs, of neurons. Spiking neurons communicate with one another using
spikes of different weights.
• Membrane potential - A physical neuron acts like a capacitor in that in-
coming spikes each add a little charge to its membrane, which in turn raises
its membrane potential. When the potential crosses a critical threshold, the
neuron outputs a spike thereby draining its membrane. In simulations, the
potential is modeled as a sum which is updated on incoming spikes.
• Potential decay - Just like real capacitors leak charge at a very low rate, so
do spiking neurons. This is modeled by subtraction of a scaled version of the
membrane potential in each update cycle.
• Refractory period - After a spike, a neuron cannot spike again for a number
of update cycles called its refractory period. Its membrane potential may still
be updated.
• Synapse/Axon - In a biological brain, the axon is an ”arm”-like part of
a neuron which transmits spikes out of the neuron, whereas the synapse is
the chemical channel between a source neuron’s axon and one or more sink
neurons. In simulation, this is modeled by a matrix of weighted connections,
and the two terms are used interchangeably.
• Excitation - Excitation means increasing the membrane potential of a neu-
ron, i.e., the result of receiving a spike from a synapse with a positive weight.
• Inhibition - Inhibition is the opposite of excitation.
• Time step - The mammal brain operates in continuous time, whereas sim-
ulations of SNNs are inherently discrete with short constant-length update
intervals. Everything in an SNN therefore happens with reference to a spe-
cific time step; spikes generated in time step i are used for updates in time
==PAGE:10==
viii
Terminology
step i + 1; if a neuron spikes in time step i and has a refractory period r it
cannot spike again before time step i + r etc. Time steps are typically 1 ms
long.
• Spike encoding - A transformation of traditional (integer/floating-point)
data representation to a binary time-indexed format suitable for an SNN.
2. Acronyms
• AER - Address event representation
• ANN - Artificial neural network, i.e., a layered network of classic artificial
neurons
• ASIC - Application-specific integrated circuit
• ASM - Algorithmic state machine
• BUFGCE - (Xilinx primitive) Global clock buffer with clock enable
• Chisel - Constructing hardware in a Scala-embedded language
• CI - Continuous integration
• CNN - Convolutional neural network
• CPU - Central processing unit
• CRV - Constraint-random verification
• FCNN - Fully-connected neural network
• FF - Flip-flop
• FFNN - Feed-forward neural network
• FFT - Fast Fourier transform
• FIRRTL - Flexible intermediate representation of RTL
• FPGA - Field-programmable gate-array
• FSM[D] - Finite state machine [with datapath]
• GPU - Graphics processing unit
• GRU - Gated recurrent unit
• GSCD - Google speech commands dataset
• HDL - Hardware description language
• IF - Integrate-and-fire
• JDK - Java development kit
• JTAG - Joint test action group (name of standardized testing interface)
• LIF - Leaky integrate-and-fire
• LSTM - Long short-term memory
• LUT - Look-up table

==PAGE:15==
CHAPTER 1
Introduction
Artificial neural networks (ANNs) are seeing ever-increasing popularity and are being
deployed to mobile devices for many different applications including image recognition
and keyword spotting. Keyword spotting (KWS) is particularly interesting to consider,
as the neural networks used for this particular application are running all the time.
Examples of such applications are Apple’s Siri, Google’s Assistant, and Amazon’s Alexa,
which are activated by saying different phrases. The KWS networks thus serve as an
early filtering of incoming audio signals such that costly connections to online audio
processing servers are opened only when absolutely needed. As such, the networks must
be relatively small and must be evaluated in efficient hardware such that they do not
drain the mobile device’s battery too quick.
The majority of research into this topic focuses on regular ANNs, which base them-
selves on multiply-accumulate operations for summing weighted inputs and non-linear
activation functions. However, such networks map poorly to the traditional Von Neu-
mann architectures employed in modern CPUs due to their memory requirements and
by relying on expensive floating-point multiplications and additions [Bou+19]. Efficient
accelerator hardware for such ANNs does exist and takes a near-memory computing
approach to distributing memory modules and evaluation units across a chip to exploit
locality in memory access patterns better [Che+14; Par+17]. However, the architectures
are inherently limited by the required arithmetic circuitry, and while there is extensive
work in reducing the power consumption of these types of networks by quantization, net-
work pruning, and approximate arithmetic [HMD16], further optimizations are possible
by switching to a class of networks known as spiking neural networks (SNNs).
SNNs are the third generation of artificial neural networks. They aim, in part, to
reduce the arithmetic requirements of neural network evaluation by reducing continuous
activations to binary events in time known as spikes, and in part, to model biological
brains more closely [Kas19]. As such, the neurons of consist of elements similar to those
of biological neurons, and as incoming values are binary, require no multiplications to
be evaluated. This enables highly efficient hardware accelerators to be implemented and
deployed in ultra low-power devices.
This report presents the design and implementation of a number of power-focused
optimizations of an SNN hardware accelerator and its conversion from MNIST til Google
Speech Commands classification. The description begins by introducing the necessary
background information related to ANNs and SNNs and the Chisel HDL used to describe
the accelerator hardware and its testing in chapter 2. Chapter 3 presents the dataset and
the network development process which precedes the hardware development. Chapter 4
==PAGE:16==
2
1 Introduction
gives a brief introduction to the baseline system which is sought optimized, while chap-
ter 5 presents the optimizations introduced. Chapter 6 covers implementation-specific
details of interest, code details and the extensive verification suite used to maintain
accelerator functionality. Chapter 7 presents the results of the optimizations both in
terms of resource usage and power consumption of the accelerator, and in terms of SNN
classification accuracy. Finally, chapter 8 discusses the work presented in the report
and potential future work, and chapter 9 concludes. All of the relevant source code is
available in the appendices A thru D.
==PAGE:17==
CHAPTER 2
Background
This chapter covers basic theory about both regular artificial neural networks, spiking
neural networks, and the Chisel HDL which is used to define the hardware accelerator
in this project. This background knowledge is the foundation of the remaining chapters
of this report. Note that this is only a brief introduction to the aforementioned topics,
and that both ANN and SNN research and development of Chisel are on-going processes.
Please refer to the listed references for more in-depth explanations.
2.1
Neural Networks
Neural networks are machine learning models of the human brain. They are directed
graphs with layers of neurons grouped together with weighted connections (synapses)
between the layers. In the following, the traditional second-generation and relatively
newer third-generation neural network models are briefly introduced. First-generation
neural networks with binary weights and activations are, effectively, no longer used and
will not be covered further.
2.1.1
Artificial Neural Networks (ANNs)
Second-generation ANNs are based on continuous neurons. As an example, consider
the simplest ANN model, a fully-connected network (FCNN), shown in figure 2.1 - fully-
connected referring to the fact that each neuron in layer L has a connection to all neurons
in layer L + 1.
Each neuron calculates a so-called activation, which is a weighted sum of the neuron
values of the previous layer and their relatively weights. The activations of the input
layer are equal to the input values, which are usually normalized to be in the range
[0, 1]. This implies that the input layer has the same number of neurons as there are
values in an input (e.g., 784 for classic 28x28 MNIST images). Similarly, the output
layer traditionally has the same number of neurons as there are classes.
The m’th neuron in layer L calculates:

be non-linear as otherwise, the network could be collapsed into a simple matrix-vector
or matrix-matrix multiplication. For classification tasks, the softmax activation func-
tion, shown in equation 2.1, is used for the output layer such that activations represent
probabilities of each class, while other layers typically use either hyperbolic tangent
(equation 2.2) or rectified linear unit (ReLU) (equation 2.3), because they provide good
network performance. ReLU is particularly popular for networks used in edge devices
due to its simplicity and good generalization performance.

When created, the network weights are typically randomized for simple initialization.
Subsequently, the network must be trained, i.e., fitted to a specific task. This can be
done in two ways depending on the task at hand; either supervised or unsupervised.
Supervised training is by far the most common as it is used in traditional classification
tasks and bases its learning on comparing a network response to an expected result
==PAGE:19==
2.1 Neural Networks
5
(e.g., the class of an image) - thus, the training data should have class labels. Unsuper-
vised learning tasks typically aim at recreating the input from a learnt smaller internal
representation - thus, training data does not need class labels. Nonetheless, this train-
ing phase must be completed prior to deployment of neural network models in their
evaluation phase.
The training process is based on backpropagation algorithms, which approximates
a gradient descent method in which the gradient of an loss (error) function between
expected output and current network output is calculated. The network output is, as
described above, a function of network weights and biases as well as the activation
functions meaning that it is possible to propagate the error back through the network
and evaluate how much of the total error each weight and bias contribute. Subsequently,
the network weights are updated towards a minimum of the loss function. The process is
extremely compute-heavy and therefore typically takes place on HPC servers or special
tensor processor hardware. When the network is deployed, it is no longer trained, but
only used for inference. The method is only approximate because since the activation
functions are non-linear, they are non-differentiable [Nie15].
The network shown in figure 2.1 is an example of a so-called multilayer perceptron
(MLP) with a simple neuron model. More complex network types (especially RNNs)
utilize more complex cells with built-in memory and more than one input and one output.
Long short-term memory (LSTM) and gated recurrent unit (GRU) cells are examples of
such cells. They are useful for networks meant to operate on longer sequences of data as
they can accumulate information internally and thus present both a current and historic
activation for the network to learn from [HS97; Cho+14].
2.1.1.1
Advantages
ANNs have undergone significant research for years at this point, and there exist several
well-developed open-source tools to model and train such networks (e.g., FaceBook’s
PyTorch [Ya] and Google’s TensorFlow [Aa]).
This also means that there are well-
known models and strategies for solving various modeling tasks; FFNNs or convolutional
networks (CNNs) for classification tasks, recurrent networks (RNNs) with memory and
backward connections for series data tasks, Transformers for natural language processing
etc. [Vas+17]. Additionally, there exist multiple highly optimized training algorithms.
So far, ANNs represent the vast majority of neural network applications.
Well-known models for image classification tasks include the VGG-16 and ResNet
family of networks used for image classification of the large-scale 1000-class ImageNet
dataset. Both use tens of layers of various types and thus billions of operations per
inference to achieve high classification accuracy [SZ15; He+15]. However, such models
are not suitable for small-scale low-power environments, but may serve as inspiration
for smaller networks.
==PAGE:20==
6
2 Background
2.1.1.2
Disadvantages
The main disadvantage of ANNs is a combination of their increasing size with task
difficulty combined with the fact that their operation depends on large numbers of
multiplications and additions. Despite being trained with floating-point numbers, the
models are typically quantized to, for example, 16-bit integers before deployment. This
is done for two reasons; it reduces the model size to about half of the original model,
and integer arithmetic is much simpler and less power and time consuming than floating-
point arithmetic. The cost, naturally, is a small drop in model accuracy, which in most
cases is acceptable if the original model performs more than well enough. Alternatively,
models that show little variance in their weights may be compressed efficiently by, e.g.,
run-length or Base-Delta-Immediate (B∆I) coding [Pek+12].
Within the scope of this work, the main disadvantage is the power consumption of
the networks - even when implemented in special hardware. The fact that inferences in
even small networks require several hundreds of thousands of multiplications is simply
not acceptable in small, power-constrained devices. Seeing as this is a general issue of
ANNs, it calls for research into alternative network models.
2.1.2
Spiking Neural Networks (SNNs)
Spiking neural networks are the third generation of artificial neural networks, which
attempt to both reduce the complexity of calculating network activations and model bi-
ological brains even closer by replacing the floating-point/integer activations with binary
events in time known as spikes.
Biological brains are analog in nature and operate asynchronously in time based on
continuous inputs. Modeling continuous inputs with infinite resolution is impossible, so
instead a constant-length time step resolution ∆t is used. This means that all layers of
the network are evaluated at each of such steps and resulting spikes are buffered and
used in subsequent time steps.
SNN research is carried out in two branches; the neuroscience branch focusing on
modeling the brain as closely as possibly, and the so-called neuromorphic (meaning using
VLSI architectures to model neuro-biological systems) computing branch focusing on
attempting to exploit the SNN features for machine learning. Naturally, the neuroscience
branch utilizes more complex neuron and synapse models to better understand how the
brain works, while the other branch simplifies the neuron and synapse models and bases
its work on models taken from existing ANN research to make training and evaluation
computationally simpler. Existing work has some overlaps between the two fields and
therefore shows great variety in neuron models, synapse models, and data encoding as
well as experimental setups. This project considers only the neuromorphic computing
branch of the field.
Consider as an example the SNN shown in figure 2.2, which is the SNN equivalent of
the ANN shown in figure 2.1. At the high level, the network consists of three elements;
a spike encoding, a neuron model, and a synaptic model. The spike encoding determines
how input data is mapped to spike trains and thus determines the output of the input
==PAGE:21==
2.1 Neural Networks
7
Input
Hidden
Output
Spike encoding
Neuron 
model #1
Neuron 
model #2
Figure 2.2: An example SNN with three layers with spike encoding and neuron models
marked. The weighted synaptic model is used. Neuron models #1 and #2 can be the
same.
layer of the network. The synaptic model determines how spikes are modeled between
neurons in the network. Most networks simply use weighted connections as in regular
ANNs, while some networks also apply a trainable delay to the synapses, which may alter
spike patterns of subsequent neurons when used in combination with decay. Finally, the
neuron model determines how a specific neuron is updated and its behavior when spiking.
There exist several different ways of encoding input data into spike trains and mul-
tiple different neuron models. The models most relevant to this project are covered in
the following sections.
2.1.2.1
Spike encoding
Spike encoding refers to the mapping process from continuous numbered data to a spike
train representation suitable for inputting into an SNN. As mentioned earlier, there are
many different ways of performing this mapping with different characteristics. The main
distinction is between rate-based and timing-based encodings.
Rate-based encodings are the most common and most intuitive. A rate-based en-
coding represents input values as spike trains in which the time distance between two
spikes is inversely proportional to the input value. This means that large input values
==PAGE:22==
8
2 Background
are represented with many spikes and small input values with few spikes. In simple
rate-based encodings, the distance between spikes is constant.
Timing-based encodings are less common but can provide even sparser activity by
representing inputs with fewer spikes overall. Typically, a single input datum is repre-
sented as a single spike whose timing in the total evaluation time carries a weight - the
larger the input datum, the earlier the spike is generated. In rank-order encoding, the
entire input is considered at once and spikes are generated based on the sorted order of
input values - greatest value first.
Figure 2.3 shows examples of simple rate encoding and rank-order encoding for three
neurons and their respective input values. The displayed time is n · ∆t corresponding to
the total evaluation time.
Input value
Rate encoding
Rank-order encoding
Neuron
1st
2nd
3rd
Figure 2.3: Examples of simple rate encoding and rank order encoding.
When training a network, one aims for the model to generalize as well as possible.
For ANNs, this often implies making minute random changes to the inputs in order to
avoid training the network on exactly the same data multiple times. A similar technique
is applied when training SNNs, where the randomness is introduced by small changes
in spike rate or relative timing of neurons. An example is Poisson encoding, which is
a rate-based encoding in which the distance between spikes is drawn from a Poisson
distribution rather than constant.
2.1.2.2
Neuron models
Neuron models differ from the two research branches. The neuroscience branch typically
uses highly detailed models based on a number of experimentally found differential
equations modeling the neurons as close to their natural behavior as possible.
The
neurocomputing branch instead focuses on computational efficiency and less on biological
accuracy.
Nonetheless, all models share a common feature; namely the modeling of a membrane
potential measuring how excited a neuron is.
When a neuron receives a spike, the
membrane potential is increased by a certain amount depending on the intensity (weight)
of the spike. The analog equivalent of this is to consider the membrane potential as a
capacitor which integrates incoming current over time to store up charge. Whenever
the membrane potential crosses a neuron-specific threshold, the neuron produces a spike
==PAGE:23==
2.1 Neural Networks
9
and its membrane is drained. Afterwards, the neuron cannot spike for a given refractory
period, which for SNNs is a number of time steps. The leaky nature of biological neurons
(and capacitors) is modeled as a decay of the membrane potential over time.
Depending on the intensity of each spike, the increase in membrane potential can be
either excitatory (i.e., making the neuron more excited) or inhibitory (i.e., making the
neuron less excited). In practice, using a combination of excitatory and inhibitory con-
nections between neurons can reduce spike activity. Additionally, some neuron models
also include a bias term which is added to the membrane potential in the end of each
evaluation - just like in regular ANNs.
Some commonly used neuron models are the Hodgkin-Huxley, Leaky-Integrate-and-
Fire, and Izhikevich models. The Hodgkin-Huxley model is a biologically plausible model
which bases its estimates around three differential equations modeling experimentally
described sodium, potassium and leakage channels of squid’s neuron. A spike is modeled
as a flow of ions from the neuron. Seeing as it is a rather detailed model (despite not
being somewhat simplified), it is preferred in the neuroscience research branch but not
so much in the neurocomputing branch.
The simplest neuron model used in neurocomputing is the integrate-and-fire (IF)
model, which simply models a leak-free membrane potential which is increased on in-
coming spikes and reset when a spike is generated. This basic model is known as leaky
integrate-and-fire (LIF) when extended by a decay factor. The continuous version of
LIF is also described by a differential equation for the membrane potential as a function
of incoming currents over time (i.e., similar to a parallel connection of a capacitor and
a resistor). The equation may be discretized and evaluated on a time step basis. It
is the most well-known spiking neuron model due to its simplicity and computational
efficiency.
Finally, the Izhikevich model combines some of the biologic plausibility of the Hodgkin-
Huxley model with the computational efficiency of the LIF model. This is achieved by
extending the simple differential equation of the LIF model by some extra parameters
which affect the recovery of the neurons; i.e., how the neuron regenerates after spiking.
In the LIF model, the neuron is simply unable to spike for a period of time, which is the
same after any spike. In the Izhikevich model, the recovery is dynamic and even affects
how the membrane potential is updated.
Various models combining features of the three described above also exist. An ex-
ample is the so-called Diehl and Cook neuron model, which is an extended LIF model
with trainable threshold [Kas19; DC15].
2.1.2.3
Network evaluation
As explained before, an SNN is evaluated in discrete time steps of length ∆t. Within
each time step t, spikes generated in the previous time step t −1 are used for updating
the relevant membrane potentials and new spikes may be generated. The new spikes are
used in the subsequent time step t + 1. This implies that spikes take a number of time
steps proportional to the number of layers to propagate through the network.
==PAGE:24==
10
2 Background
For a specific neuron, the membrane potential is updated by all spikes from neurons
to which it has synapses. The size of each increase depends on the weights assigned to
the synapses over which the spikes were transmitted. Once updated by all spikes, the
bias is added and the membrane potential is updated by the decay factor. If the potential
remains higher than the neuron’s threshold and the neuron is out of its refractory period,
the neuron spikes and its membrane potential is reset. The refractory period is kept by
a saturating decrementer.
As an example, figure 2.4 shows the membrane potential of an LIF neuron receiving
only excitatory spikes and whose bias is zero. The neuron has a refractory period of one
time step. Within the first time step, its membrane potential crosses the threshold, a
spike is generated, and the membrane potential is reset. Within the second time step,
the membrane potential once again crosses the threshold, but since the neuron is in
its refractory period, no spike is generated. At the border between two time steps, a
decay of 0.9 is applied to the membrane potential.
Please note that certain neuron
models restrict membrane potential updates to take place only when a neuron is out of
its refractory period.
The network response to a specific input is the set of spikes output by the network
over a predetermined number of time steps, n. That means, instead of presenting a
classification result as a vector of probabilities, instead the evaluation is based on average
spiking activity of neurons assigned to a specific class.

spike stimuli that leads to generation of new spikes in an LIF neuron. The neuron shown
has a refractory period of one time step. All three plots share the same x-axis. The
y-axis of the middle plot is normalized w.r.t. the neuron’s threshold potential.
==PAGE:25==
2.2 Chisel
11
2.1.2.4
Advantages
The list of advantages of SNNs is long. Their learning capability is the same as for other
ANN types, while they are significantly easier to evaluate. Their main advantage is that
updates to a neuron’s membrane potential is simple addition or subtraction rather than
the multiply-accumulate operation of regular ANN neurons. This means that neurons
can be implemented in hardware with significantly less area and thus power than their
ANN counterparts.
The typically sparse spiking activity is another benefit when considering custom
hardware accelerators, because it enables equally sparse interconnect between neurons
in the network. Additionally, the use of spikes as asynchronous events in time makes
SNNs even more suited for hardware acceleration. This has already been explored by
both Intel and IBM in their respective Loihi and TrueNorth neuromorphic chips, which
implement thousands of spiking neurons with support for dynamic configuration as well
as interconnection of multiple chips [Dav+18; Ako+15].
2.1.2.5
Disadvantages
Currently, the main disadvantage of working with SNNs is the relative youth of the
research field and thus its tools. Although several open-source tools (for example; nengo
[Bek+14], ANNarchy [VDH15] and BindsNET [Haz+18]) exist, they are usually based on
the well-establish existing ANN tools, PyTorch and TensorFlow, which are optimized for
regular ANNs running on GPUs or TPUs. Modeling SNNs efficiently on these platforms
is not ideal and leads to long training and evaluation time. Thus, rather than modeling
SNNs directly, some tools instead train regular ANN models and implement a kind of
conversion step to transform them into SNN equivalents; e.g., by using the snn-toolbox
package [Rue+].
Training is further complicated by the fact that SNNs tend to have many more high-
level parameters (evaluation time, time step length, reset potentials, thresholds, decay
factors, refractory periods etc.)
as compared to their ANN counterparts.
Although
the majority of the parameters are constants and thus untouched by training, finding
the optimal combination is difficult. Additionally, existing training algorithms based on
STDP are inherently unsupervised and perform poorly compared to well-known ANN
counterparts [Tav+19; Bou+19].
2.2
Chisel
Chisel1 is a modern HDL by UC Berkeley and CHIPS Alliance (funded by Google,
Intel, SiFive etc.) [Law+; Ea]. It is embedded in Scala which is based on Java, but
enables native support for functional programming features. Chisel cannot be compiled
directly to, for example, an FPGA, but instead it is first mapped to an intermediate
1Any mention of Chisel refers to the Chisel3 Scala package.
==PAGE:26==
12
2 Background
representation called FIRRTL which is further transformed (emitted) to synthesizable
Verilog or SystemVerilog. Being a primarily academic tool thus far, the Verilog constructs
generated from it are made to map well to both Xilinx and Altera FPGAs. The following
is a short introduction to component definition and testing with Chisel.
2.2.1
Modules
In Chisel, components are defined as classes that extend the base Module class. Modules
must define a field called io which includes a bundle of signals defining the IO of the
particular module. This bundle also includes the definition of an implicit clock and
reset. Subsequently, the class’ body, representing its constructor, defines the module’s
hardware with a number of constructs.
All operations are based around three data
types; Bools representing Boolean values, UInts or SInts representing unsigned and
signed integers, respectively. In practice, Bools can be used the same way as VHDL’s
std_logic type, while UInt is used like a combination of VHDL’s std_logic_vector
and unsigned types.
Hardware can be constructed with all the typical Boolean operators, as well as with
a number of constructs representing either Wires, rising-edge triggered active-high reset
registers, multiplexers or synchronous/asynchronous memories. As an example, consider
the following code snippet, which is an implementation of the pipelined adder shown in
figure 2.5.
1
class PipelinedAdder(width: Int = 32) extends Module {
2
val io = IO(new Bundle {
3
val en = Input(Bool())
4
val op1 = Input(UInt(width.W))
5
val op2 = Input(UInt(width.W))
6
val res = Output(UInt(width.W))
7
})
8
9
val en
= Reg(Bool())
10
en := io.en
11
12
val op1 = RegEnable(io.op1, 0.U, io.en)
13
val op2 = RegEnable(io.op2, 0.U, io.en)
14
val add = Wire(UInt(width.W))
15
add := op1 + op2
16
17
val res = RegInit(0.U(width.W))
18
when(en) {
19
res := add
20
}
21
io.res := res
22
}
==PAGE:27==
2.2 Chisel
13
Note that the design above could be written in a much more concise style, but it
is left rather verbose for illustrative purposes.
A Verilog version of the module can
be emitted with the following code and by using the SBT command sbt "runMain
PipelinedAdder".
1

Additionally, Chisel supports blackbox components either with or without Verilog
source code. Such modules either extend BlackBox or ExtModule and enable emission of
custom modules with a specific structure. This allows users to reuse prior implementa-
tions directly in their Chisel project or include placeholder instantiations of source-less
blackboxes such as FPGA primitives. For example, the following is a definition of a
source-less Xilinx BUFGCE primitive [Sup18]:
1
class ClockBufferIO extends Bundle {
2

= Input(Clock())
3
val CE = Input(Bool())
4
val O
= Output(Clock())
5
}
6
7
class BUFGCE extends BlackBox(Map("SIM_DEVICE" -> "7SERIES")) {
8
val io = IO(new ClockBufferIO)
9
// empty constructor body
10
}
Hierarchies of modules are created in the same way as in other HDLs by instantiating
new copies of sub-modules. The benefit of Chisel is particularly clear in this regard as
its functional features allows instantiation of, for example, eight of the above modules in
a single line as follows. Combined with features such as the bulk connection operator <>,
this makes for fewer lines of code as compared to similar VHDL or Verilog descriptions.
1
val adders = Seq.fill(8) { Module(new PipelinedAdder) }
2.2.2
Testing
Testing, or verification, of Chisel modules is enabled primarily through the early alpha
ChiselTest package developed by the same team as Chisel and FIRRTL [La]. ChiselTest
is based on the ScalaTest package meant for software regression/unit testing and enables
users to run simulation-based verification flows based on so-called testers (or specs). The
tests are described with four basic operations; peek, poke, expect and step operating
on IO of the DUT. In particular, peek enables viewing the value of an output, poke
enables setting a particular value on an input, and expect represents an assertion-based

14
2 Background

io_en
io_op2
0
0

clock
+

0

io_res
en

check of a specific value on an output. step operates on the DUT’s clock and advances
simulation time by one clock cycle. As an example, consider the following tester of the
design presented above:
1

Per default, tests are run based on FIRRTL’s native simulation engine, Treadle [Ma],
but for larger systems tests, the backend can be switched over to either Verilator or
Synopsys VCS. Verilator support is more extensive than support for VCS primarily due
to the fact that Verilator is open-source [al]. Tests are also per-default run without any
kind of output except a few printed lines in the terminal window, but it can be configured
to output VCD results. Using Verilator and outputting VCD results is enabled as:
1
...
2

3

4

==PAGE:29==
2.2 Chisel
15
5
// test body
6
}
7
}
8
...
ChiselTest also supports multithreaded tests with fork and join. This is similar to
VHDL testbenches’ parallel processes and is particularly useful for testing components
with enqueue-dequeue functionality like, e.g., FIFOs. Tester threads are synchronized
on calls to step to ensure correct operation.
2.2.3
Advantages and Disadvantages
Chisel is still undergoing development, and so two of the main disadvantages are that it
lacks features as compared to VHDL and (System-)Verilog (e.g., sub-vector assignments),
and that its API may undergo relatively large changes rendering older designs to require
significant modifications to be updated. Seeing as it is open-source, many more people
can help suggest and develop features to make them available in the future; thus, the time
from feature suggestion to implementation in the public snapshot repository is relatively
short. Additional disadvantages include a lack of support for non-edge triggered latch-
based storages and limited multi-clock support; issues which have affected this project.
The main advantage of Chisel is that it abstracts away some implementation details
(in particular, modules have implicit clock and reset signals) from the user, making
Chisel code very concise. Being completely separate from relevant testing features, it
is not possible to mix non-synthesizable code into correctly written Chisel code, as it
is in both VHDL and Verilog. Additionally, being embedded in Scala, users can make
full use of the object-oriented and functional programming schemes to generate complex
hardware with little code. This also makes CRV much easier in ChiselTest as compared
to VHDL or Verilog testbenches, and at a much smaller initial effort than using, e.g.,
UVM [Dob+21].
The interested reader may refer to the free e-book [Sch19], the related GitHub repos-
itories [Law+; Izr+; Ma] or the Chisel homepage [Ea].
==PAGE:30==
16
==PAGE:31==
CHAPTER 3
Network Development
This chapter describes the Google Speech Commands dataset (GSCD) and the prepro-
cessing steps performed before training an SNN model on it. Next, it introduces the
showcase network that is implemented using the BindsNET library in Python and ex-
ported and executed on the accelerator. Finally, it presents a regular PyTorch ANN
reference model used primarily to verify that it is possible to train a model on the
preprocessed dataset. Throughout the project, the models have also been trained and
tested on the MNIST dataset as a baseline for how well they learn.
Training scripts are included as separate files included in appendix A listings A.4 and
A.5. All other Python source files are organized as a library kwsonsnn also included in
appendix A. Some of the exploration is documented in the shape of a Jupyter notebook,
which is not included in the appendix, but available in the Github repository.
3.1
The Google Speech Commands Dataset
Google Speech Commands is an open dataset for speech recognition by Google Brain
[War18]. More specifically, it targets keyword spotting (KWS) - the operation of contin-
uously listening for trigger words before launching a full-scale speech recognition service.
Most well-known examples include Google’s ”Ok Google”, Apple’s ”Hey Siri”, and Ama-
zon’s ”Alexa”. The unit of recognition is generally a single word or a very short phrase.
KWS models should be small and have low compute requirements as they are supposed
to be on all the time. Additionally, they are expected to primarily operate on noise or
silence, which should not activate the full-scale service. In the following, the dataset is
first introduced followed by an explanation of the preprocessing steps applied to it.
3.1.1
Dataset Description
GSCD is available in two versions; the first version used in this project contains 64,727
utterances spread across 30 different keywords, and the second version used in recent
research contains 105,829 utterances spread across 35 different keywords.
All audio
files are roughly one second long and come from many different speakers as they were
crowdsourced through an app and an online collection tool. Both versions of the dataset
are distributed through the TensorFlow project [War18; Wara; Warb]. Class labels are
provided in the form of directory names, while files meant for validation and test splits
are listed in two .txt files. Per default, the dataset employs an 80-10-10 split.
==PAGE:32==
18
3 Network Development
The keywords of the first version are selected from different relevant fields; firstly, to
match other research, the dataset includes utterances of digits from zero to nine (similar
to spoken MNIST and TIDIGITS [LD93]); secondly, words relevant to the IoT or robotics
fields like ”up”, ”down”, ”on” and ”off”; finally, the remaining words are random nouns
or names some of which are similar in pronunciation to some of the others, e.g., ”tree”.
The second version dataset simply extends the first with more robotics-related keywords
like ”forward” and ”backward”. An additional set of files include recordings of various
types of background noise.
In the work related to this project, authors limit themselves to the set of 10 IoT/robotics
keywords, and thus, the same is done in this project [Zha+18]. Additionally, authors
choose to include ”unknown” and ”silence” classes to underline their network’s perfor-
mance in those special cases; i.e., show that it does not flag those classes unnecessarily.
The accelerator presented in this project is designed to work with 10 classes, and thus,
to include unknown and silence classes as part of the keyword spotting application, two
of the 10 keywords were discarded; ”left” and ”right”. These two words were removed
due to their lesser relevance to the hearing aid field than the other eight keywords. The
final list of classes is thus:
up
down
on
off
yes
no
go
stop
unknown
silence
Figure 3.1 shows the distribution of samples in each of the 10 classes in the different
splits. The data for the plots is generated in the Jupyter notebook using the custom
dataset class.
3.1.2
Preprocessing
To easily use the dataset in Python, it should be wrapped in a class. Luckily, BindsNET
includes an implementation of the spoken MNIST dataset that can be used as a reference
point [Haz+18]. The resulting SpeechCommandsDataset class is available in appendix A.

==PAGE:33==
3.1 The Google Speech Commands Dataset
19
Each of the audio files goes through a number of preprocessing steps before being
included in the final dataset. The audio is sampled at 16 kHz with samples represented
by 16-bit integers. Firstly, when fetched, the audio signals are padded to a length of one
second with white noise sampled from a normal distribution scaled by the median of the
original signal. The majority of the subsequent processing steps find their inspiration in
[Fay16] and BindsNET’s spoken MNIST implementation, see here.
Next, the signal is pre-emphasized and normalized; the first of which aims to amplify
high frequencies to make the frequency spectrum more balanced. The resulting signal
is cut into frames of a suitable length and overlap. The preprocessing targets images
of 22x22 pixels matching the scaled-down MNIST images used in [Rib20]. The frame
overlap is traditionally between 0 and 50%. Using a frame length of 85 ms and a stride
of 40 ms leads to 1 s−0.085 s
0.040 s
≈23 frames of which the last one is not used. The frames
are passed through a short-time Fourier transform (STFT), which is a frame-based
fast Fourier transform (FFT) variant [PM07]. The resulting power spectrum is further
transformed into a so-called mel-frequency cepstrum (MFC). In an MFC, frequency
bands are equally spaced on a mel scale rather than a linear frequency scale as in the
FFT. The transform from frequency to mel is the following:
m = 2595 log10
(
1 + f
700
)
(3.1)
This representation approximates human hearing more closely [Fay16]. Finally, the filter
banks are converted to dB and normalized. The resulting ”images” resemble regular
spectrograms with time on the x-axis and frequency on the y-axis.
A literature review showed that related works tend to use a frame length in [10, 40] ms
and a frame overlap in [0, 75]% indicating that the initially selected values are too large
[Zha+18; Blo+19; Sun+16]. Additional experiments also showed that especially SNN
models had a hard time learning from the processed data. This was likely due to two
phenomena:
1. Different utterances of the same word by different speakers might have drastically
different MFCs either due to high background noise levels or due to the position
of the utterance in the audio snippet
2. The relatively low resolution of the MFC leads to high pixel values and thus high
spike rates when using rate encoding or seemingly randomly timed spikes when
using rank-order encoding
In an attempt to solve these issues and concurrently reduce the frame length, the pre-
processing was altered to include a section of code that calculates the dot product of
8000-sample snippets of the signal taken at a stride of 1000 samples with a Blackman
window of the same size. The absolute value of the snippets is used for comparison and
the frame with the highest dot product is selected. The Blackman window was selected
for two reasons; it is available directly in Python’s Numpy library, and its graph is sim-
ilar to that of a normal distribution, see the middle of figure 3.2. The second reason is
==PAGE:34==
20
3 Network Development
most important as it ensures selection of snippets whose loudest sections are centered.
Although barely noticeable, the final waveform in figure 3.2 has its peaks centered better
compared to the original waveform.
The resulting 8000-sample frames now represent only half a second of audio. Thus,
it is possible to use a frame length of 40 ms and a frame stride of 20 ms (leading to
25 frames of which the last three are not used), which is closer to that of related work.
Finally, a filter that sets all pixels whose values are less than the 80th percentile of the
MFC to zero was added. This was done to isolate the most important pixels. The
80th percentile was chosen as roughly 20% of pixels are non-zero in scaled-down 22x22
MNIST images, which [Rib20] proved a small showcase network could learn from.
Although the above optimizations lead to more similar MFCs and higher classification
accuracy, different utterances of the same keyword still, as expected, show some variation.
Consider, for example, the four utterances of ”up” shown in figure 3.3. The utterances are
reasonably centered and have similar-looking waveforms, and their MFCs show similar
patterns albeit with some differences. The most noticeable differences are the delayed
pop-like pronunciation of the letter p in the third example and the powerful low-frequency
background noise in the fourth example, which sets them apart from the others.
Loading the dataset from individual audio files is very memory intensive. To solve
this, when each split is fetched the first time, a number of files in the Pickle format are
generated within the relevant keyword folders. Pickle is a Python library that utilizes
zip-like compression algorithms to store Python objects to files. The Pickle interface to
Python is very intuitive and thus part of both Numpy and PyTorch.
A similar methodology is used for both silence and unknowns; silence samples are
generated only once per split independent of the keywords used and stored within a
new folder _silence, while unknown samples are dependent on the keywords used and
thus generated again if a new keyword combination is passed to the class. To enable
efficient reuse, unknown samples are stored in files whose names are hashes of the lists of
keywords used in the samples in a new folder _unknown, see listing 3.1. For simplicity,
all Pickle files store a triple of audio signals, labels and the sampling rate used.
Original signal
Weight function
Result signal

sulting from centering. The x-axis of the left-most plot has twice the range (in [0, 16000))
of the other two plots (both in [0, 8000)). The audio snippet amplitudes are normalized
to [−1, 1], while the window function lies within [0, 1].

3.2
The Showcase Network
The project bases its work around a showcase network for which the accelerator is de-
signed. It is a small FFNN-like SNN model adapted from an existing model described in
[DC15] with some adaptations matching those presented in [Rib20] and the supervised
MNIST example available as part of BindsNET. In the following, the network architec-
ture is presented, followed by an in-depth description of its development in BindsNET,
and the classification results achieved. Finally, the development of a reference PyTorch
model used for sanity checks of GSCD is presented.
3.2.1
Network Architecture
The showcase network is shown in figure 3.4. It is a fully connected SNN with three
layers; an input layer X, an excitatory layer Ae which acts both as an internal layer and
==PAGE:36==
22
3 Network Development
as the output layer, and an inhibitory layer Ai which acts to inhibit the activity of the
Ae layer. The input layer has 484 neurons, thus taking in the 22x22 images produced
by preprocessing the dataset, and the other two layers both have 200 neurons. Layer
Ae uses Diehl and Cook neurons, while layer Ai uses basic LIF neurons. The network is
trained using rank-order encoding with no randomization in the input layer.
Only the weights between layers X and Ae are trained, while the other weights are
constants scaled by exc and inh set in the training script. All trainable weights are
in the range [0, 1], and they are divided by a constant value set in the training script
after each update to keep them low. Weights from the Ae to the Ai is a square diagonal
matrix, i.e., WAe_to_Ai = exc · I200. This means that a spike from layer Ae excites only
one neuron in layer Ai. On the other hand, weights from the Ai to the Ae layer are a
non-diagonal matrix, i.e., WAi_to_Ae = (inh · I200) −inh. An inhibitory neuron thus
inhibits all neurons in Ae except the one with the same index.
Note that figure 3.4 also includes the sizes of the weight matrices and the IDs assigned
to the axons, which are relevant for mapping the network to an accelerator.
X
Ae
Ai
484x200
[0:483]
[0:199]
[512:711]
200x200
200x200
484 neurons
200 neurons
200 neurons
Figure 3.4: The showcase network architecture. Number of neurons and axon IDs are
shown in the figure. They match the ones shown in [Rib20].
Just as described in [Rib20], the network is trained using floating-point numbers, but
the accelerator operates on fixed-point numbers. Thus, the weights must be converted
before being mapped to the accelerator. This is described more in-depth in the following
chapters.
==PAGE:37==
3.2 The Showcase Network
23
3.2.2
Development in BindsNET
The network implementation in BindsNET is available in appendix A listing A.3. The
implementation is near identical to that of the DiehlAndCook2015 model included in
BindsNET, see here, with minor changes to the default values of some parameters as
well as changes to some neuron specifics forcing them to use positive membrane potentials
and thresholds.
The network differs from the one presented in [Rib20] in that its weights and thresh-
olds are unscaled and thus represented by small floating-point numbers. Additionally,
minor changes to the inhibitory connection weights, learning rates, and weight normal-
ization have been introduced in an attempt to achieve better classification accuracy.
The model is trained by using the separate training script available in appendix A
listing A.5. The training script may be called with a set of command line options, all
of which have default values that make the script run with minimal compute effort; i.e.,
not necessarily the best setup for, e.g., evaluating a network with new parameters. The
parameters are listed in table 3.1.
The script is clearly separated in three sections as most such scripts are; namely, an
argument passing section in which the parser and its options are declared and assigned
to suitably named variables, a setup section where the model and datasets are declared,
and the training loop.
Within the setup section, the model is first declared with the selected parameters.
Next, depending on the --use_mnist option, either the MNIST or GSCD dataset is
loaded and passed into a so-called DataLoader, which helps to more efficiently load data
into the relevant memory prior to its use. Next, the spike encoders are declared. Per
default, the script uses BindsNET’s RankOrderEncoder for both training, validation and
testing images, and the NullEncoder for labels. When a rate encoding is to be used, a
PoissonEncoder should be used for training images and a RateEncoder for validation
and testing, as explained in section 2.1.2.1. Finally, training and plot related variables
are declared and initialized accordingly. If the --gpu option is passed and a GPU is
available, the network and dataset are moved to it. Assignment updates and accuracy
calculations are kept on the CPU due to an error otherwise being thrown by BindsNET’s
all_activity(...) and proportion_weighting(...) functions.
The training loop is built around a classic PyTorch structure. It is a for loop running
the requested number of epochs. Within each epoch, the entire training set is iterated
over and used for training with updates and plots in regular intervals as requested, and
the validation set is used to check training progress at the end of each epoch. The script
implements checkpointing, which serves to always keep track of the best-performing
model. If at the end of an epoch, the current iteration of the model outperforms the
previously best-performing model in validation accuracy, a copy of it is stored.
Training is based on the simple PostPre algorithm, which is an STDP-based algo-
rithm which aims to ensure the same spiking activity in the post-synaptic neuron as in
the pre-synaptic neuron. As explained in section 2.1.2.5, STDP algorithms are inher-
ently unsupervised. The training script bypasses this limitation by clamping one (or
more) neuron(s) in the excitatory layer such that weights between the spiking neurons

At the end of the training loop, the test set is iterated over to check the model’s

possible to halt the training at any point and not lose any work.
For the majority of short experiments run as part of this project, the following
command was executed:
python train.py --plot --epochs 1 --n_train 1000 --n_valid 100
[--intensity 37.0]
For longer training runs, the following command was used:
python train.py --gpu [--intensity 37.0]
In both cases, the --intensity argument may be left out when using rank-order
encoding. When using rate encoding, an intensity of 37 ensures the same average spike
activity when using GSCD as when using MNIST with an intensity of 128 as in [Rib20].

The networks trained as part of this project all perform significantly worse compared
to the ones presented in [DC15; Rib20]. In general, no iteration of the network develop-
ment achieved better than 50% classification accuracy, which is well below what is to
be expected from a system of this kind. This poor performance might be explained by
one or more of the following reasons:
• the showcase network is too simple both in architecture and in the number of
trainable weights, or
• the BindsNET library does not have training algorithms capable of capturing the
patterns of GSCD when using either rate or rank-order spike encodings, or
• the GSCD images are of too low resolution to be learnt efficiently by any network.
The latter reason is unlikely as other related works use similar-sized images and achieve
much better results; albeit, using different network architectures and/or different SNN
libraries [Zha+18; Wan+20]. Experiments with using the DiehlAndCook2015 network
1Training models with BindsNET is extremely slow compared to regular ANN training. Each image
can take between 0.3 and 1 second to process, even on GPU.
==PAGE:40==
26
3 Network Development
architecture with corresponding rate encoding and scaled-down MNIST images in Bind-
sNET show that the model still fails to achieve near the results presented in [DC15].
Unfortunately, no other works use the same network architecture for GSCD classifica-
tion, and BindsNET does not have any reference results to compare with, meaning that
the first two reasons remain likely.
Throughout trying to optimize network performance, the most important plot pro-
duced by the training script is the receptive field (square weights) plot which shows the
weights arranged in 22x22 squares for each neuron. The interesting observation is that
when training a network for MNIST, the weights form clear patterns that resemble the
digits they are trained for. Looking at the same type of plot for GSCD instead shows
that there is similarity between same-class examples, but also that there is little varia-
tion across classes. No such plot is included here2, but one may be produced using the
training script in appendix A listing A.4.3
A further discussion of solutions to this issue and experiments to carry out as future
work is presented in chapter 8.
3.2.4
PyTorch Reference Model
A PyTorch reference model is included in its training script, see appendix A listing A.5.
It is a small FFNN with the same dimensions as the showcase network, except it is
non-spiking and has trainable weights between all layers. The ReLU activation function
is used out of the hidden layer, while the softmax activation function is used out of the
output layer to make it output probabilities. Being so simple, it fits in very few lines of
code as shown in listing 3.2.
Since the network has 200 output neurons, the probabilities output are summed in
groups of 20 neurons after which the greatest sum represents the network prediction,
again to mimic the operation of the showcase network.
The loss function used for
training the network also implements this summation and returns the mean absolute
error between the summed probabilities compared to a one-hot encoded expected value.
From a few experiments, this seemed to give better results than mean squared error.

table 3.3. MNIST classification results are available in appendix A table A.2. Training is
carried out using the Adam optimizer with a start learning rate of 0.0002. The numbers
clearly show that it is possible to learn patterns from the GSCD, but that some classes
are much more difficult to predict than others. This was the expected result judging by
the relatively poor classification accuracy of the BindsNET model. As explained earlier,
the issue is that the preprocessed utterances from GSCD are very similar across different
classes - especially the unknown class is troublesome as some of its keywords are easily
mistaken for other classes.
Another observation to be made here is that it is possible for relatively small networks
to learn patterns from the preprocessed GSCD. Hence, the dataset carries only part of
the blame for the poor observed performance when using SNN models, as explained in
section 3.2.3.

CHAPTER 4
Original Accelerator
This chapter first gives a brief introduction to the design presented in [Rib20] followed
by a short discussion of its main shortcomings. All circuit diagrams shown here and in
the following chapters use the same style meaning that thin wires represent single bits,
while bold wires represent bundles whose widths are greater than one bit.
4.1
Accelerator Design
The original accelerator design targets the computational SNN field, and more specif-
ically power-constrained devices used in hearing aids or IoT devices. Thus, the main
focus points are low power consumption while ensuring the required functionality of
a useful system. As such, the accelerator per-default only supports the group of LIF
neurons (including Diehl and Cook neurons) and a limited number of synapses between
different network layers. The design is modular and, to some degree, parameterizable
with configuration of number of evaluation units etc. Configurations have to satisfy
a set of requirements ensuring correct network evaluation within each time step. The
design takes inspiration both from state-of-the-art designs such as IBM’s TrueNorth
chip [Ako+15], Intel’s Loihi chip [Dav+18], as well as several others [Raj+19; Fan+18;
Bou+19].
4.1.1
Top-level Overview
The design is a near-memory computing-inspired accelerator based around a configurable
number of cores of three different types; input cores to act as the input layer of the im-
plemented network, neuron cores taking care of evaluating the neurons of the network,
and output cores to extract generated spikes from the accelerator. To enable easy test-
ing on an FPGA, the top-level design implements an off-chip communication module
based around a UART. The accelerator bases its evaluation around a two-phase system
controlled in part by the off-chip communication and the accelerator cores such that the
cores are always operating in the same phase and recently input spike periods do not
affect the current inference.
The accelerator thus employs the common architecture of evaluation cores communi-
cating over a fabric of sorts [Raj+19]. This is illustrated in the abstract overview shown
in figure 4.1a. Figure 4.1b shows the structure of the implemented accelerator. This
figure also shows how the accelerator targets a modular implementation in which in the
==PAGE:44==
30
4 Original Accelerator
number of input, output, and neuron cores may be controlled to match a corresponding
network. The main modules of the system are introduced in more detail in the following
sections.

Figure 4.1: 4.1a is an abstract overview of the common way of arranging neuromorphic
accelerators, while 4.1b shows the design of the accelerator in [Rib20].
4.1.2
Spike Communication
The accelerator employs the basic zero-delay synaptic model, and seeing as it is rather
small, the communication fabric implemented is a simple bus, as shown in figure 4.1b.
All cores are equipped with a bus interface, and input and neuron cores also include a
spike transmission system modules capable of converting internal spike formats to the
one used on the bus. The bus is a bundle of shared wires OR’ed together in the top-level
module1, and spikes are communicated using an AER encoding of coreID # neuronID
[Sta02]. Each bus interface includes a LUT-style ROM with an entry for each other core.
The ROM is used for filtering spikes from the bus such that only spikes whose core IDs a
core is subscribed to are passed through to the core’s so-called axon system. Conflicting
bus requests are handled by a round robin bus arbiter with lookahead.
The axon system is relatively simple combination of two memory modules and two
counters. The two modules are time-multiplexed and switched between in each time step
such that one memory is being written to, while the other is being read. This ensures
the correct operation between two consecutive time steps. Combined with the commu-
nication fabric and network weights, the axon system implements what is known as a
neuromorphic crossbar. An example of this structure is shown in figure 4.2. Incoming
spikes enter the crossbar from the left, and dots represent weighted connections that
1Chisel does not support tri-state signal values. All cores per default output zero on the bus.
==PAGE:45==
4.1 Accelerator Design
31
scale the spikes before they are output. In the accelerator, incoming spikes are stored in
their AER format and used for addressing memories storing the weights that are then
used to update neurons’ membrane potentials. More on this in the following sections.
axons
neurons
synapses
mapping
Figure 4.2: An example neuromorphic crossbar, as inspired by [Fan+18].
4.1.3
Spike Input and Output
Spikes are input into the accelerator through input cores, which perform simple opera-
tions on an indirectly encoded image whose pixel values represent spike periods. As such,
the input cores implement a bus interface and a spike transmission system to communi-
cate correctly with the communication fabric, an FSM and a pair of time-multiplexed
memories for storing images. The off-chip communication module writes spike periods
into one of the memories, while the other is being read by the FSM.
Similarly, spikes generated by the core which accelerates the excicatory layer, are
intercepted on the bus and output to the off-chip communication by an output core.
like the input core, it is a relatively simple core implementing a bus interface and a
small FIFO queue to store incoming spikes. As such, it implements no FSM but rather
just the read and write control logic for the FIFO queue.
4.1.4
Neuron Evaluation
Spikes generated by the input cores are evaluated by a set of neuron cores.
These
cores implement a discretized version of the LIF neurons described by three equations
[Bou+19]. When doing so, two neuron values can be updated in each time step; the
membrane potential and the refractory counter. Evaluated membrane potential is up-
dated according to equation 4.1, which describes the membrane potential of neuron j of

The accelerator implements this using a number of different modules. As shown in
figure 4.3, the neuron cores include instantiations of a bus interface, a spike transmission
system, and a neurons block, which again contains eight neuron evaluators and corre-
sponding evaluation memories controlled by a common FSMD. The neuron evaluators
each evaluate a subset of the neurons mapped to the specific core in a time-multiplexed
manner.
The transmission system implements an arbitration scheme to fairly select
between spikes in the rare occasion of two concurrently evaluated neurons in a core
spiking simultaneously. All network-related values including the dynamic neuron values
are stored in the evaluation memories using an offset-based addressing combined with
the AER of the bus. This underlines the focus on modularity. Network parameters are
scaled and rounded to 17-bit signed integers, which the neuron cores operate on.
==PAGE:47==

4.2
Shortcomings
The accelerator has a few shortcomings, some of which are also pointed out in [Rib20].
Firstly, its memory requirements are rather large due to the way the evaluation mem-
ories are implemented. In particular, the fact that neither their size nor their width is
a power of two (or a size that is easily optimizable by Vivado) means that they are
difficult to map to BRAM cells on the FPGA. In the original design, the evaluation
memories contain all relevant information for each set of time-multiplexed neurons; i.e.,
refrac counters, membrane potentials, weights, biases etc. The high memory consump-
tion significantly contributes both to the fact that a Xilinx Kintex-7 FPGA is needed
to fit it, and to the accelerators second shortcoming; its power consumption.
Secondly, being designed for low-power applications, the accelerator’s power con-
sumption is nearly 1 W in an FPGA and estimated 65.9 mW in an ASIC implemen-
tation. This is way too much for a hearing aid use case, in which the steady power
consumption should rather be close to or less than 5 mW. The power consumption
is, as reported in chapter 6 in [Rib20], dominated by three components; static power,
dynamic power in BRAMs, and dynamic power in the clock distribution network. Static
power is mainly dominated by the sheer area of the accelerator and thus depends both
==PAGE:48==
34
4 Original Accelerator
on the number of BRAM modules and other primitives used by the accelerator. The
dynamic power, on the other hand, is highly dependent on the switching activity in the
accelerator, and the routing of the clock network.
==PAGE:49==
CHAPTER 5
Design
This chapter introduces the new accelerator design and the updated and new modules. It
begins by defining the baseline system which is to be optimized, introducing the design
objective and the approach taken. Next, an overview of the final design is presented
followed by in-depth descriptions of the optimizations introduced and changes to system
modules accompanying them. Any modules not mentioned in this section are, apart
from potential code optimizations, the same as presented in [Rib20]. Implementation
details are available in the following chapter.
5.1
Baseline and Design Objective
This section gives a brief introduction to the baseline system configuration used as basis
for the optimizations introduced, and the objective of the optimizations. Further details
follow in the upcoming sections.
5.1.1
Baseline Design
The baseline design used for understanding the effects of the introduced optimizations
is the design for accelerating the showcase network described in [Rib20]. In particular,
the version of the accelerator implementing the so-called ”cheat” to reduce the number
of globally unique axon IDs to 768 instead of 1024. This ”cheat” was introduced as
the evaluation memories would otherwise become too large for Vivado to efficiently map
them to BRAM cells. This is due in particular to their non-power-of-two size. As a
result, the ”cheat” version of the accelerator has some axons with overlapping IDs but
still operates correctly because of the implemented coreID # neuronID AER. This
”cheat” is kept in place to provide a fair basis of comparison.
As such, the baseline design implements two input cores covering 484 input neurons
(out of 512 possible), two neuron cores with 200 neurons mapped to each (out of 256
possible), and an output core that outputs spikes produced by the excitatory layer. The
entire accelerator operates on a common 80 MHz clock produced by an on-chip MMCM.
==PAGE:50==
36
5 Design
5.1.2
Design Objective
The design objective is two-fold; first, to carry over the main design objectives presented
in [Rib20], i.e., modularity and parameterization; and second, and most important, to
reduce the power consumption of the accelerator with relatively few but well-founded
changes. As such, the overall structure of the original accelerator and the majority of its
modules remain; albeit, some with slight alterations and others completely redesigned.
The power optimizations are introduced in two orthogonal steps:
1. High-level clock gating
2. Spike encoding change and network quantization
These two steps and the module changes introduced as part of them are presented
in more detail in the coming sections. Both are motivated by the shortcomings of the
original accelerator design outlined in section 4.2 and the list of potential future work
presented in [Rib20].
The main motivations behind introducing clock gating were firstly to explore the
potential effects of this, as the original design only employs inferred clock gating, and
secondly, to reduce the switching activity both in the accelerator cores and the corre-
sponding clock network when the cores are idle. This introduction is expected to have
a significant impact on the power consumption of the accelerator seeing as its cores are
idle in almost 99% of the time [Rib20].
Changing to a timing-based spike encoding can, as briefly discussed in chapter 2,
reduce network activity and thus the power consumption of the accelerator cores during
evaluation. Furthermore, switching to a different encoding affects only the input cores.
Similarly, quantizing network weights to a different and smaller format than the original
accelerator’s 17-bit signed integer format can reduce the size of the evaluation memories
and, thus, the accelerator area resulting in a reduction in both static and dynamic power
consumption.
Furthermore, an implicit design objective is to reduce the number of BRAM cells as
much as possible, as they seem to dominate both area and power consumption of the
original accelerator. This focus has led to small, but not insignificant changes introduced
to both spike period memories and evaluation memories.
5.2
Design Overview
The structure of the accelerator remains the same after optimization, but the cores are
decoupled from the top-level control after introduction of an extra clock domain. The
clocks of the two clock domains are, of course, correlated, and thus it should be possible
to perform timing closure around them with little trouble. Nonetheless, the accelerator is
designed to have as few signals as possible crossing the clock domain borders. Figure 5.1
shows an overview of the final system with all of its modules. With parameterization, it
is possible to select the number of neuron cores P, but the system per default implements
==PAGE:51==
5.3 Clock Gating
37
two input cores and one output core to fit the showcase network. Note that for simplicity,
some control signals for time step signaling, the phase system and clock gating signals
produced by the cores are left out in the overview.

the two clock domains; the top clock domain and the core clock domain.
All of the modules shown in figure 5.1 that have changed as compared to the baseline
design are presented in more detail in the following sections. Initially, changes were
introduced to implement clock gating, and subsequently to change the spike encoding
and network parameter representations to reduce spike activity and save storage space.
5.3
Clock Gating
This section describes the high-level clock gating approach, its control, and the design
changes it introduces.
==PAGE:52==
38
5 Design
5.3.1
Approach
Clock gating may be introduced at multiple levels of the design all the way from switching
off entire sections of a design to just a single or a few cells. The high-level (coarse-grained)
clock gating approach1 was selected for several reasons. Firstly, it requires relatively few
changes to the accelerator. Secondly, it is well-suited for this application due to the
evaluation model employed by the cores in which they are all active in a short while in
the beginning of each time step after which they are all idle. And finally, the FPGA used
for testing the design has limited clock enable-equipped clock buffers available [Sup18]
making lower-level (fine-grained) clock gating more difficult to implement2.
The implemented clock gating switches the entire accelerator off once all cores have
finished evaluating their spikes of the current time step till the next time step begins.
This is achievable by subtle changes to the top-level module. The central component is a
gated clock buffer (BUFGCE primitive for Xilinx FPGAs [Sup18]) and its control logic
with corresponding control signals passing both ways over the clock domain border as
indicated in figure 5.2. The cores based around FSMs are given two extra IO ports; an
input newTS used to signal that a new time step is beginning, and an output pmClkEn
indicating that a core still needs the clock to be enabled. The newTS signals are generated
from a common time step cycle counter in the top-level, while the pmClkEn signals are
generated based on evaluation status in each core - more on this in the following sections.
The pmClkEn signals are combined in the top-level module using the saturating counter
shown in figure 5.3, which helps to ensure that pending bus operations are completed
before the clock is switched off. Currently, the value x is set to 50 cycles, which is plenty
to flush all bus interfaces.
CE
