Figure 5.3: The circuit which combines incoming pmClkEn signals from all the cores and
disables the clock after a x cycles have passed since no core had its pmClkEn asserted.
The BUFGCE primitive is a global clock buffer with a CE signal built-in, which
operates in the expected way of a clock gating cell.
That is, if the enable signal is
asserted while the clock is high, the clock is not activated before its next rising edge.
Similarly, if the enable signal is deasserted while the clock is high, the clock pulse is
still completed. Hence, no glitches or untimely clock pulses will affect the accelerator
cores [Sup18]. Unfortunately, the Xilinx manuals do not describe potential clock delay
introduced by the BUFGCE, so to balance the potential skew between the gated and
non-gated clocks, an extra BUFGCE with its enable input always asserted is instantiated
in the top level. This extra BUFGCE drives the always-on modules.
Note, that, traditionally, disabled cores should not require incoming control signals
for clock gating, but rather they should be put in an idle state before being disabled,
such that they resume correct operation once the clock is re-enabled. However, such an
implementation is more suitable for per-core clock gating instead of the global approach
taken here. It is also important to note that while the system now implements a gated
clock domain, it still shares a common synchronous reset signal. The functionality of
the MMCM and BUFGCEs is such that upon a reset, the clock is propagated to all cells
regardless of the clock enable CE signal shown in figure 5.2 [Sup18].
5.3.2
Core Decoupling
The introduction of high level clock gating means that the system now has two separate,
albeit correlated, clock domains. Thus, the two domains should be decoupled from one
another as much as possible; i.e., as few signals as possible should cross the domain
border in order to minimize the risk of timing issues causing metastability and to make
the system easier to synthesize as fewer signals need to undergo timing closure.
In the original design, spike periods are fed into the input cores which contained
time-multiplexed dual-port memories, and spikes are output through a simple FIFO
[Rib20] included in the output core. The easiest approach to decoupling the accelerator
is thus to replace these memories by true dual-port equivalents with two independently
==PAGE:54==
40
5 Design
clocked interfaces, as shown in figure 5.1. Both FPGAs and ASICs may be configured
to implement such memories with little overhead [Sup19].
5.3.2.1
Organization
To make this decoupling simpler, the memories are moved out of the input and output
cores and instantiated as part of the top-level module to make it easier to control their
clock connections. Orthogonally, the decision was made to replace the time-multiplexed
memory system of the input cores with an offset-based addressing system. The reasoning
was that the memories are relatively small and combining them halves the number of
BRAM cells required to implement them at little hardware overhead. The phase system
described in section 4.1.1 remains and is now only used to select whether to add an
offset to the requested address. The input core and off-chip communication operate out
of phase such that they do operate on the half of the memory used by the other. Note
also that the data-out port facing the off-chip communication and the data-in port facing
the input cores are not used as either module only performs writes or reads, respectively.
Additionally, if the number of neurons mapped to a single core is a power of two, the
offset addition may be replaced by a simple OR operation.
5.3.2.2
True Dual-port Memories
The true dual-port memory module is a rather primitive module and thus not shown in
a figure. Instead, it is more interesting to focus on the FIFO as there are several ways
to implement a true dual-port FIFO. Initial attempts had multiple issues particularly in
terms of correctly indicating full/empty status. Generating such flags requires passing a
version of the write address and read address across the clock domain border and perform
some comparison between them on either side. This is nearly impossible to do by passing
unsigned integer addresses across the border - regardless of whether synchronization
registers are used. Instead, most implementations employ a Gray encoding which is one
bit wider than the address counters and has several benefits over the unsigned addresses.
Firstly, the inherent benefit of Gray code is that only one bit changes in the bit pattern
following an increment, potentially leading to fewer metastability issues. And secondly,
the redundant format enables simpler generation of the full/empty flags by comparing
in particular the MSBs of the two codes; keeping in mind that the MSB of an n-bit Gray
code follows the same pattern as the MSB of an n-bit binary code [Chu06; Gis18].
The FIFO used as part of this project finds its inspiration in an implementation
available in [Chu06] section 16.9.1 and utilizes the true dual-port memory module pre-
viously described. The design is presented in more detail in figure 5.4. The read and
write control logic is placed in each their own clock domain, and no synchronization
registers are placed between them. They share many of the same features, but their flag
generation hardware differs slightly. Both sides operate in a cyclic fashion following the
bit patterns generated by the Gray-encoded read and write pointers. In the figure, the
==PAGE:55==
5.3 Clock Gating
41
left side of the dashed line contains logic clocked by the write clock, while the other side
is clocked by the read clock.
The increment block shown in figure 5.4 represents a Gray code increment opera-
tion, which is most easily handled in three steps: (1) Gray-to-binary conversion, (2)
binary increment, and (3) binary-to-Gray conversion. The Gray-to-binary and binary-
to-Gray conversions are based on XOR operations. An n-bit Gray code (gn−1, ..., g1, g0)
is converted to a corresponding n-bit binary code (bn−1, ..., b1, b0) as follows:
bn−1 = gn−1
bn−2 = bn−1 ⊕gn−2
...
b1 = b2 ⊕g1
b0 = b1 ⊕g0
Thankfully, in Chisel this is quite easy to implement in a single line of code:
VecInit((0 until n).map( i =>c(n-1, i).xorR()).toSeq).asUInt
The binary-to-Gray conversion is much simpler. Using the same n-bit binary code as in
the example before, the corresponding Gray code is calculated as follows:
(gn−1, ..., g1, g0) = (bn−1, ..., b1, b0) ⊕(0, bn−1, ..., b2, b1)
Finally, the binary increment is a simple unsigned addition.
The implemented queue is relatively small with just 16 entries. This should be plenty
as some initial experiments using the provided pre-trained showcase network showed
that the excitatory layer only produces roughly 100 spikes over the duration of an entire
inference, all of which are read and transmitted by the off-chip communication module
as they are produced. Additionally, as reported in [Rib20], 16 entries was enough for the
queue to never fill up, even when using rate encoding. For 16 queue entries, n = 4, and
the read and write pointers are 5 bits each, as shown in figure 5.4. The read and write
addresses used to access the dual-port memory consist of the XOR of the two MSBs
concatenated with the n −1 LSBs of the counters. With this in mind, the queue is full
when the addresses are the same, but the MSBs of the pointers differ, and it is empty
when both the addresses and the MSBs of the pointers are the same.
5.3.3
Other Module Updates
Introducing the true dual-port memories presented above leads to changes to several
other modules; most importantly, the off-chip communication, and the input, neuron,
and output cores. The updates to these modules are explained in more detail in this
section.

5.3.3.1
Off-chip Communication
The off-chip communication remains roughly the same as in [Rib20], except the address-
ing logic for the input memories has changed from the time-multiplexed interface used
in the baseline design, and the logic to read out spikes from the output queue is slightly
different. As such, the off-chip communication still instantiates a UART and a control
FSM for receiving spike periods from a computer and writing the received periods into
the input memories, as shown in figure 5.1. The FSM also manages the phase system
that ensures synchronicity between the state of the off-chip communication and the ac-
celerator cores. Periods are still received as four bytes each; first, two bytes representing
the input neuron ID, which acts as an index into the memories, followed by the spike
period also represented by two bytes. All four values are shifted into four registers addr1,
addr0, period1 and period0 when received via the UART. Subsequently, as only 256
neurons are mapped to each input core, addr1 is used only for selecting which input
memory to write the spike period to. The ASM chart for the control FSM is shown
in figure 5.5. Notice how the FSM signals that it is ready to receive a new image by
transmitting 255, a value that lies outside the range of possible output spike IDs with
the showcase network’s 200 neurons in layer Ae.

5.3.3.2
Input Core
The input core module is updated in several ways. Firstly, its previously built-in time-
multiplexed memories have been removed, as mentioned earlier. Secondly, its FSMD has
undergone a little change to implement the clock gating. Finally, it no longer contains
its own time step cycle counter but instead utilizes a shared counter included in the top
level. Regardless of the changes, its main mode of operation remains the same. As in
[Rib20], the input core is still limited to supplying spikes to as many neurons as the
neuron cores can fit, i.e., 256 for the current implementation. This ensures conformity
with the AER protocol employed on the communication fabric.
==PAGE:59==

Figure 5.7: The control circuitry for reading the output FIFO and communicating spike

The input core’s datapath still contains both a transmission system and a bus inter-
face. As will be covered later, the new input core implements rank-order encoding, but
still uses the indirect period-based encoding to generate spikes. Similarly, as mentioned
earlier, the phase handshaking between the off-chip communication and the input core
to ensure correct operation when switching between inferences is still in place. Figure 5.8
shows the ASM chart of the input core FSMD. It resembles that of [Rib20] significantly,
although some signals differ and the original sWait state has been replaced by a done
state with different operation.
Per default, the input core’s pmClkEn signal is asserted, while memEn (i.e., the enable
signal for the input memories) is deasserted. For clarity, figure 5.8 shows the value of
pmClkEn in all states, as it can be deasserted depending on various conditions in two of
the five states. memEn, on the other hand, is only shown when it is asserted. The state
machine resets to the idle state from which an inference is begun. The firstr state
serves to enable the synchronous input memory such that its first output is available
when entering the spikegen state in which all but one pixels may be turned into spikes.
Once enabled for all input pixels, the state machine moves to the lastgen state in which
it may generate the last spike before entering the done state. Here, the FSM will spend
the majority of the time when it has finished generating spikes and is waiting for the
next time step of an inference. It only returns to the idle state after a full inference
and stays there till a phase change is signaled by the off-chip communication and a new
time step has begun.
Spikes are generated the same way as before by supplying a vector of Booleans to the
transmission system. Common subexpression elimination is applied to avoid generation
of two copies of the comparison circuit for the spike generation.
The result is the
shouldSpike signal, which is now shared between the spikegen and lastgen states.

5.3.3.3

interface has been flushed. The axon system is left unchanged, the neuron evaluator
has an 8:1 decay selection multiplexer replaced by a shift operator (likely implemented
as a barrel shifter) instead, while the evaluation memories see a larger redesign to be

presented in section 5.4.2.2.
The control unit has changed slightly; partly in terms of introducing the extra control
signals for clock gating, partly to implement the different addressing model used for the
evaluation memory optimizations, and partly as it no longer includes a time step cycle
counter but instead utilizes the shared one like the input core.
The ASM chart for
the control unit FSM is shown in figure 5.9. All of the control signals have default
values; clock gating control signal done, evaluation memory controls ena and wr, and
axon system enable aEna are all deasserted per default, while the address signals refer
to the constant memory’s address zero. The evaluation units are enabled per default,
but disabled in the idle state in which the control unit spends the majority of the time.
The FSM is in its idle state at the beginning of each time step. When a new time
step begins, it updates each of the neurons mapped to the core eight at a time, one per
evaluation unit. The evaluation is controlled by a bundle of control signals for each pair
of evaluation unit and corresponding memory.
To better understand the ASM chart, the signal names presented in it must be de-
scribed. Firstly, sel and pos refer to evaluation memory module selection and address as
is described in more detail in section 5.4.2.2; const, dynamic, biasthresh and weights
refer to enumerated values used for increased code readability. The inOut register imple-
ments a phase system similar to that between the off-chip communication and the input
cores, except it is used to select between the time-multiplexed memory modules of the
axon system. This register must be switched at the beginning of a new time step to read
out spikes generated in the previous time step. Similarly, the spikeCnt register stores
the number of spikes received by the axon system in the previous time step and affects
how many iterations of the rWeight1 and rWeight2 states to execute, if any, before
passing on to reading and applying biases. The aData signal refers to the output of the
axon memories and represents a neuron ID which is used for offsetting indexing into
the evaluation memories. Finally, the neuron and axon registers are used for counting
which set of eight neurons is currently undergoing evaluation and for indexing into the
axon system memory, respectively. For simplicity, the bundled control signals for the
evaluation units are abstracted into textual descriptions of their values in each state.
Despite the memory optimizations introduced in the following section, the evaluation
memory still has only one output interface to the evaluation units. Thus, the number
of cycles to pass over all steps to evaluate a layer of neurons remains to be memory
bound. As such, each state of the control unit still enables only one interaction with
the evaluation memories. The states remain named describing their functionality; states
with a prefixed r enable the memories for reading, while states with a prefixed w enable
the memories for writing. Their individual functionality is the same as described in
[Rib20] section 3.7.2.3 and will thus not be covered in greater detail here.
5.3.3.4
Output Core
The output core sees only little change apart from the removal of the FIFO queue and
its control logic. The resulting core is very small and implements only a bus interface,
==PAGE:63==
5.4 Architectural Changes
49
write enable logic for the true dual-port FIFO, and the necessary clock gating hardware.
The resulting system serves the same purpose as originally; it reads spikes from the
communication fabric using its bus interface and communicates them to the off-chip
communication through the FIFO. It differs from the input and neuron cores in that it
does not include any FSM. Hence, it does not require a newTS input and its pmClkEn
signal must be generated in a different way. As such, this is done by an OR reduction
of the bus OR’ed with its bus interface’s valid signal. The resulting implementation is
shown in figure 5.10.

5.3.3.5
Neuromorphic Processor
Finally, the top-level neuromorphic processor module is updated to include the afore-
mentioned time step cycle counter to generate the control signal that re-enables the
core FSMs and causes them to re-enable the core clock. Additionally, it instantiates all
of the modules shown in figure 5.1 and places them in their respective clock domains.
The time step cycle counter remains a saturating decrementer set to count down from
80 MHz · 1 ms = 80000 and asserting newTS for a single cycle when reaching zero.
5.4
Architectural Changes
This section presents the architectural changes introduced to support rank-order en-
coding instead of rate encoding in the input cores, and the quantization of network
parameters.
5.4.1
Spike Encoding
Changing the spike encoding in the input core proved to require relatively little work.
As presented in the previous chapter, an input image is fed into the accelerator in
==PAGE:64==
50
5 Design
an indirect spike format; namely, spike periods. Using rate encoding, each pixel can
lead to multiple spikes during an inference, and thus, the circuitry required is a costly
modulo operator and a time step counter. The rank-order encoding approach uses a
similar indirect format of input images, except the pixel values no longer represent spike
periods, but rather which time step the particular pixel should spike in. This also means
that the modulo operator may be replaced by a simple comparator.
Figure 5.11 shows a comparison of the hardware required for either implementation.
Apart from the expected power savings arising from the lower spike activity in the
input cores and subsequently the other cores resulting from the different encoding, a
comparator is also a significantly smaller circuit than a modulo operator. Thus, an area
saving is expected, although it is unlikely that this will lead to a significant power saving
as the power is dominated by memory accesses, and the number of accesses does not
change. Changing the encoding does not affect the input core’s state machine.
=
shouldSpike
period
ts
0
%
(a)
=
shouldSpike
period
ts
(b)
Figure 5.11: Hardware for spike generation for: 5.11a rate encoding, and 5.11b rank-
order encoding.
5.4.2
Weight Quantization
The work presented in [Rib20] trains a network whose parameters are scaled by a factor
of 1000 such that they are easily quantizable to the 17-bit integer format used in the
accelerator. This approach does not match that of most recent works which instead train
unscaled networks and quantize the weights to a suitable fixed-point format depending
on the acceptable loss in network accuracy [HMD16; Den+20]. The following sections
present the parameters meant to be stored in the evaluation memories, the selection of
suitable fixed-point formats, as well as the updated evaluation memory design.
5.4.2.1
Network Parameters
To better understand the quantization options available, it is important to understand
the parameters required to fully describe the showcase network - essentially, the param-
eters that are included in the evaluation memories. The memories store the following
parameters (number of entries per evaluation memory in the original accelerator in
parentheses):
• Reset potentials (32)
• Refractory periods (32)
• Decays (32)
• Biases (32)
==PAGE:65==
5.4 Architectural Changes
51
• Thresholds (32)
• Weights (32 · 768 = 24576)
• Refractory counters (32)
• Membrane potentials (32)
Reset potentials, refractory periods, and decays are constants for each layer, however,
the original accelerator allocates memory space for unique values for each neuron. This
provides a small optimization opportunity seeing as just one of each parameter could
be shared between all neurons mapped to a specific evaluation memory. This, of course,
may reduce the set of networks that can map to the accelerator to ones whose layers
share these three parameters, but it does not affect networks defined in BindsNET.
Biases, thresholds, weights, refractory counters, and membrane potentials, on the
other hand, are unique for each neuron and thus must have adequate space allocated
for them. The refractory counters and membrane potentials are the only dynamic pa-
rameters of the neurons implementation; i.e., the only values which are updated by the
neuron evaluators. Notice in particular how the evaluation memories are dominated by
the weights pinpointing them as being the most relevant for optimizations.
The parameters have very different numerical ranges. The decays, refractory periods
and counters are usually relatively small integers; at least when employing the shift-
subtract estimation decay technique used in [Rib20]. Membrane potentials, thresholds,
biases and weights, on the other hand, are floating-point values in the software model
with much greater potential range. With the exception of thresholds, decays, and the
trainable weights, the remaining parameters are controllable and may thus be selected
to fit specific fixed-point formats. In the experiments carried out as part of this project,
thresholds and constant weights have been controlled in increments of 0.5, i.e., values
that may be perfectly represented by fixed-point formats with just one decimal bit. This
means the fixed-point format is primarily determined by the number of bits required to
represent the trainable weights.
This project takes a conservative approach to quantization due to limited oppor-
tunities for evaluating the effects of different quantizations in BindsNET. Hence, the
approach chosen is to introduce a fixed-point format of the same width as the original
memory entries for all network parameters except the weights. As a result, the design
targets a 7.10 format which strikes a good balance between supporting suitably large val-
ues with relatively good accuracy to three base-10 decimals. Different formats are used
for different parameters due to their numerical ranges, and the formats are converted
on-the-fly as needed.
As such, it is reasonable to limit reset potentials, decays and refractory periods to
small 5-bit integers, the first of which must be converted to 7.10 to be used in the neuron
evaluators. Biases, thresholds and membrane potentials may be kept directly in the 7.10
format. Weights may be grouped in two; the trainable weights and the constant weights.
These may be stored using two different formats indicating the different numerical ranges
they take. Hence, trainable weights are stored in a 0.10 format suitable for their [0, 1]
range, while constant weights are stored in a 6.4 format giving them greater range but
less precision. Weights are stored in 11-bit entries, and the MSB of each weight memory
entry is used to select between the two formats.
==PAGE:66==
52
5 Design
Using these formats and selecting the default network parameters listed in table 3.1,
only quantizing the trainable weights – and potentially biases, if they were non-zero –
introduces error. For the pre-trained network included in the project’s Github repository,
this error amounts to just 7.0·10−4 on average. Expectedly, this should have little effect
on overall network accuracy as NNs generally are known to be quite error resistant – this
is underlined by the number of papers focusing on NN compression that show little effect
of more aggressive quantization approaches than that taken here [HMD16; Den+20].
5.4.2.2
Evaluation Memory
The updated version of the evaluation memories exploits the optimization opportunities
outlined above and implements a split memory organization with parameters grouped
according to their widths as follows (selection enum in parentheses):
• Reset potential, refractory period, decay (const)
• Refractory counters and membrane potentials (dynamic)
• Biases and thresholds (biasthresh)
• Weights (weights)
The design is shown in figure 5.12. The control unit FSM of the neuron core is altered
to implement a split address protocol in which two bits addr.sel are used to select
between the four memories, and a wider addr.pos signal is used to address the memories.
Switching to this alternative addressing mode requires little change to the control unit
FSM and no change to the interconnect defined in the neurons module.
The diagram shows how only one of the memory modules is enabled at once controlled
in part by the shared input en and the value of addr.sel.
A pipelined version of
addr.sel is used to connect the requested memory module to the output. Furthermore,
the diagram shows how weights are kept in two formats selected between by the most
significant bit of each entry and how they are appended other bit sequences to match
the 17-bit format used in the neuron evaluators. Similarly, as described earlier, the
reset potential is stored in a different format from the refractory period and the decay,
and thus, a pipelined version of the two least-significant bits of addr.pos is used select
between the two formats. The changes to the control unit are indicated its ASM chart
in figure 5.9.
The previous implementation of the evaluation memory module required a total
storage of:
(7 · 32 + 24576) · 17 b
1024 b/kb
= 411.7 kb
This fits into roughly 17 BRAM cells depending on placement and cell duplication. The
new implementation reduces the storage requirement to:
4 · 5 b + 2 · 64 · 17 b + 24576 · 11 b
1024 b/kb
= 266.1 kb
==PAGE:67==

Figure 5.12: An overview of the evaluation memory implementation. The four memory
blocks share the majority of top-level IO ports and are selected between using addr.sel.
Combining wires indicates concatenation. Note that the relative scale of the memory
blocks is not accurate.
The new implementation also means that, again depending on mapping, the evaluation
memory modules fit into 13 BRAM cells (11 for weights, one for dynamics, and one
biases and thresholds), assuming the constants memory will be mapped to registers or
pipelined LUT-RAM due to its size. That is a significant area reduction!
Not only does the new quantization save storage space, it also ensures that writes
affect only the dynamics memory. This, combined with the fact that significantly fewer
BRAM cells are enabled at once, will likely lead to significant power savings.
The
optimization comes at little overhead in terms of address pipelining and output selection
hardware in the evaluation units – something that likely cancels out the savings arising
==PAGE:68==
54
5 Design
from moving away from the offset-based addressing system used in the original control
units [Rib20]. Furthermore, the split memory implementation enables returning to 1024
unique axons as 1024 · 32 = 32K fits the depth of the available BRAM cells, should one
wish to do so.
==PAGE:69==
CHAPTER 6
Implementation
This chapter presents implementation specific details both in terms of code considera-
tions and testing suite. First, a brief overview of the available parameterization options
of the accelerator is presented followed by a description of the mapping process from
BindsNET model to the accelerator. Second, an in-depth presentation of the verifica-
tion flow employed in the project is presented. Finally, a short guide to building the
accelerator is given.
6.1
Parameterization
The accelerator design employs much the same parameterization options as in [Rib20],
although some parameters have been removed as a result of the changes introduced in
particular to the evaluation memories. In the original design, the accelerator could be
configured to implement any generic network with a total of 1024 globally unique axons,
but a ”cheat” was introduced as the evaluation memories would otherwise be too large
to fit the FPGA targeted, as described previously. A further discussion of the AER is
provided in section 8.3.2.
6.1.1
Available Parameters
Currently, the implementation provides the following parameters all included as global
constants as part of the neuroproc package:
• FREQ denoting the operating frequency targeted for the accelerator. This pa-
rameter affects the number of cycles per time step as well as the off-chip commu-
nication’s UART module. Defaults to 80 MHz, roughly the maximum achievable
when synthesizing the design in Vivado [Rib20].
• BAUDRATE denoting the baud rate used by the off-chip communication’s UART
module. Defaults to 115,200.
• TIMESTEP is the length of a time step in seconds. Defaults to 1 ms correspond-
ing to the value used when training the showcase network.
• EVALTS is the number of time steps to run for a full inference. This also delimits
the size of incoming spike periods.
==PAGE:70==
56
6 Implementation
• CORES denoting the number of cores implemented. This constant is used in
some loops in both the bus arbiter and top-level modules, but it does not affect
the number of cores of each type being instantiated.
• CONSTDATAWIDTH is the width of the entries in the constants part of the
evaluation memories. Defaults to 5 bits.
• NEUDATAWIDTH is the width of the entries in the dynamics and bias/thresh-
old parts of the evaluation memories. Defaults to 17 bits, as in [Rib20].
• WGTDATAWIDTH is the width of the weights part of the evaluation memories.
Defaults to 11 bits.
• AXONNR is the number of uniquely numbered axons supported. As described
above, this is currently set to the ”cheat” value of 3·256 = 768 which is sufficient to
accelerate the showcase network. With the introduction of architectural changes
to the evaluation memories, this can safely be changed back to 1024 if needed.
• TMNEURONS denoting the number of neurons to map to each neuron evaluator.
Defaults to 32.
• EVALUNITS denoting the number of neuron evaluators to implement per neuron
core. Defaults to 8.
• RANKORDERENC is used to select between rate and rank-order encoding
hardware in the input cores. Defaults to true.
• INPUTSIZE is the size of the input image. Defaults to 22 · 22 = 484 matching
the size of the preprocessed GSCD snippets.
Finally, the package contains several parameters resulting from various operations on
the above. Most noticeably are the global address width used on the bus GLOBALADDRWIDTH
= log2Up(CORES) + log2Up(EVALUNITS) + log2Up(TMNEURONS).
The original implementation from [Rib20] made these constants available in a sepa-
rate file called Constants.scala including a similarly named Scala object that all other
source files needed to import to use the constants. This implementation moves away
from this approach and instead organizes the source files in a Scala package neuroproc
with a corresponding package object available in package.scala, see appendix B list-
ing B.1. This means the constants are, per default, available to any other files in the
same package.
6.1.2
Parameterization Considerations
The parameterization options listed above only provide limited control over module gen-
eration. A fully parameterized design should be generic enough to work under almost any
configuration (that satisfies the previously mentioned correctness requirements) without
==PAGE:71==
6.2 Mapping to Accelerator
57
the need for additional changes to the source code. This is not currently the case, as, e.g.,
the mapping script is limited to work solely for the showcase network and the top-level
module also hardcoded to include the right number of cores of each type.
Furthermore, seeing as Chisel is embedded in Scala, which has native support for both
explicit and implicit parameters to class constructors, this parameterization technique
should be reconsidered.
More advanced projects, such as RocketChip, employ only
implicit parameters. They work in much the same way as global constants, but enable
much easier scoping.
6.2
Mapping to Accelerator
The mapping process from a pre-trained network to the accelerator means to take the
network parameters and distribute them into memory initialization files for each of the
evaluation memories, and to map the network layers to the accelerator cores.
6.2.1
Evaluation Memory Initialization
The process of mapping a network to evaluation memories is carried out in two steps.
Firstly, the pre-trained network is stored as a PyTorch-formatted Pickle file, which is
read into a small Python script, export.py, see appendix A listing A.7. From there, the
non-None network parameters (biases are not used for this network and thus stored as
None) are extracted one by one. Within the script, they can be scaled and rounded
accordingly after which they are written to a JSON file.
Secondly, the JSON file is processed by a Scala script, Mapping.scala, see appendix B
listing B.19. The script utilizes some of the constants describing the accelerator. The
script reads the JSON file and formats its entries as arrays of the proper data types.
Afterwards, it first produces the evaluation unit ID-independent initialization files con-
taining constants (reset potential, refractory period, and decay factor) and dynamic
variables (refractory counters and membrane potentials). The dynamic variables are all
initialized to zero. Next, it produces files containing biases and thresholds, and files con-
taining the weights. Both are dependent on the evaluation unit ID. In all cases, numbers
are scaled to their desired fixed-point precision.
This procedure differs from that presented in [Rib20] in that the networks are trained
using un-scaled weights (rather than weights scaled by a factor of 1000), such that their
dynamic range is kept low. As described in section 5.4.2, the weights should fit into two
10-bit fixed-point formats. The weights between layers X and Ae are, due to their low
dynamic range, mapped to a 0.10 format, while the other weights are mapped to a 6.4
format. For simplicity, remaining values will stay at 17 bits as in [Rib20], albeit mapped
to a 7.10 fixed-point format. The mapping script utilizes the round method to convert
from floating-point to integer numbers. This was chosen over the toInt method which
always floors input floats, as rounding will tend to give rise to a smaller overall error, as
listed in section 5.4.2.2.
==PAGE:72==
58
6 Implementation
The procedure is kept in two different languages because of their different features.
Python is easiest to use when opening Pickle files, but Scala has better support for
low-level bitwise operations.
As such, JSON only serves as a practical intermediate
representation between the two.
Note that an additional Python script, convert.py,
capable of reading an original 17-bit integer JSON file, as used in [Rib20], and convert
it into a partly floating-point equivalent that works with the mapping script is also
provided with the source code, see appendix A listing A.8.
Note that neither of the three scripts are written specifically targeting the showcase
network. They require some rewriting to become fully generic, although the necessary
functions and transforms are available.
6.2.2
Core Mapping
Mapping layers to cores is handled in the same way as presented in [Rib20]. The bus
interfaces each contain a small ROM, which is manually initialized based on a JSON
file whose entries are numbered by core IDs. The ROM entries determine whether a
specific core is subscribed to spikes with the core ID present on the bus, and – for the
neuron core to which the Ae layer is mapped – whether the spike maps to a weight in
the X_to_Ae or Ai_to_Ae connection.
Using the same network architecture, the mapping is also the same as presented in
listing 5.2 in [Rib20]. This means that the X layer remains mapped to two input cores
with IDs 0 and 1, the Ae layer is mapped to core 2, the Ai layer is mapped to core 3,
while the output core with ID 4 simply subscribes to spikes from the Ae layer.
6.2.3
Various Issues and Solutions
The issue of generating 16 different versions of the EvaluationMemory module with
different initialization files described in [Rib20] remains to be solved the same way. In
updated versions of Chisel, it might be that providing paths to the initialization files as
arguments to the class constructors will lead to generation of different modules.
Another issue that arose after altering the implementation of the evaluation memories
was that Vivado optimized the memories of core 3; i.e., the memories containing that
Ae_to_Ai weights. The connections are all initialized to either 0 or −inh because of
their constant nature described in section 3.2.1. This leads to a reduction of the memory
width to two bits per entry instead of the 11 bits used in the description; i.e., one bit
to select between the two fixed-point formats, and another to select the entry’s value.
Such an optimization would give the new accelerator an unfair advantage in both area
and power compared to the original while also removing the possibility of having greater
variation in weights in core 3. Thus, to avoid this optimization, an unused write port
was introduced to the weight memories, forcing Vivado to avoid optimizations1. Please
note that this write port is not shown in figure 5.12.
1Vivado seemingly ignores both keep and dont_touch attributes when doing memory optimizations.
==PAGE:73==
6.3 Verification
59
Finally, Chisel does include a FixedPoint data type that supports all the required
operations and implicitly checks for mismatching decimal points at compile time. This
would be highly suitable for the evaluation memories and neuron evaluators, but it is
still undergoing development and thus not yet fully documented nor part of the non-
experimental API. As a result, it was opted against.
6.3
Verification
The accelerator is provided with an extensive test suite. This test suite has served two
purposes during the project period; firstly, writing unit tests for each of the original
modules based on their descriptions in Chisel was an opportunity to better understand
their operation, and secondly, the unit tests served to ensure that nothing was broken
after updates were introduced. After the project, they serve to ensure that no future
updates to the accelerator break any of the existing modules.
While some tests were part of the original design presented in [Rib20], several modules
were missing tests, and the neuromorphic processor tester was missing checks for correct
outputs. Additionally, the ChiselTest package is set to take over from the Chisel iotesters
package used in [Rib20], and thus, this was an opportunity to switch.
Tests are included in two sub-packages to the main kwsonsnn package; namely,
unittests and systemtests. These are described in-depth in the following.
6.3.1
Unit Tests
The unit tests package includes tester classes for all modules, apart from the neuro-
morphic processor module.
Each tester runs series of inputs and expects the mod-
ules to respond in the correct manner. Whenever possible, in particular when testing
memory-based modules, basic principles of constraint random verification have been
applied. This means that all inputs are essentially random, and the method aims to
cover most of the interesting input combinations by fewer tests than required to ex-
haustively cover every combination [Dob+21; Mey04]. This is particularly clear in the
TrueDualPortMemorySpec class, included in appendix C listing C.7, where both ad-
dresses, data and enables are randomly generated.
6.3.2
System Tests
The system tests package includes testers for larger combinations of modules than what
fits in a regular unit test. The main tester is the neuromorphic processor tester, which
runs the accelerator processing a full input image, see appendix C listing C.21. This has
served to check that introduced code optimizations did not alter the operation of the
accelerator. During the initial stage of the project, the original accelerator’s response to
a reference image was recorded and has since been used as expected output.
==PAGE:74==
60
6 Implementation
The technique also led to the discovery of two errors in the source code presented
in [Rib20]. The two errors were found through a step-by-step introduction of updated
components until the test outputs differed from the expected. These two errors were:
• The original EvaluationMemory class used loadMemoryFromFile without specify-
ing the file content type. The function defaults to expecting hex format files, but
the initialization files are in binary format2. No errors are thrown when building
the accelerator or running simulations of it, but the spike activity is much higher
than what must be expected due to some thresholds having the wrong sign.
• The original OutputCore class contained a basic FIFO with a saturating items
counter which was unfortunately one bit too small. This meant the queue was
never registered as being full and would thus get entries overwritten constantly.
Both errors have been addressed to the author of [Rib20].
The system-level tests also make full use of the capabilities for testing anynomous
modules in ChiselTest. This enables creating modules only for testing and makes it easier
to check, e.g., that the output core works correctly with the supplied FIFO, and that
the off-chip communication works with both input core memory and output FIFO. For
examples, see the OutputCoretoMemTester and OCCtoMemTester classes both included
in appendix C.
6.3.3
Selection of Simulation Backend
The majority of the tests are executed with the Treadle backend as it is faster to start
up than Verilator. Verilator is used for all other tests as it is much faster when executing
larger tests. Tests of blackbox modules with Verilog source also requires Verilator.
During the project period, the implementation of clock gating led to a discussion of
whether Verilator was the right simulation engine to stick with. This discussion arose
as Verilator is a synchronous simulator rather than an event-driven simulator, as is
typically used in vendor tools such as Vivado or VCS. This means that Verilator only
to a certain extend supports non-edge-triggered storage devices, and seeing as the clock
gates are latch-based, they are not guaranteed to work properly. In fact, the Verilator
documentation states: ”Verilator is optimized for edge sensitive (flop based) designs. It
will attempt to do the correct thing for latches, but most performance optimizations will
be disabled around the latch.” on page 48 [Sny].
To ensure correct operation, one could switch to Synopsys VCS, but Chiseltest’s VCS
backend is quite limited. Initial attemps at running simulations in VCS were unsuccess-
ful primarily due to the much increased simulation time as compared to other available
tools – the top level simulation did not pass the 50% mark after more than 24 hours.
Contacting the developers of ChiselTest instead led to them giving assurance that Ver-
ilator should work correctly in this case, as it is the simulator currently employed by
2Since Chisel is undergoing development, it is likely this error is due to an API update [Law+]
==PAGE:75==
6.3 Verification
61
the developers of Rocket Chip (likely the most well-known Chisel project). Addition-
ally, they pointed out the /*verilator clock_enable*/ signal attribute that instructs
Verilator’s scheduler that a signal is used to gate a clock and that no race conditions
occur on it [Sny]. As a result, the implementation of a latch-based clock gate, shown in
listing 6.1, resembles that of Rocket Chip, available here, and uses this attribute. It is
written in SystemVerilog to enable use of the always_latch construct.
1
/* verilator lint_off UNOPTFLAT */
2
module ClockBufferBB(input I, input CE, output O);
3
reg en_latched /*verilator clock_enable*/;
4
always_latch @(*) if (!I) en_latched = CE;
5
assign O = en_latched & I;
6
endmodule
Listing 6.1: A latch-based clock gate with the Verilator clock_enable attribute to
ensure correct simulation.
Top-level tests can also be run through Vivado’s XSim using a VHDL testbench,
which is included in appendix C listing C.22. The testbench performs the same test as
its ChiselTest equivalent based on the same input image and expected output. Running
the ChiselTest tester in Verilator takes roughly two hours depending on other system
loads, while running a post-implementation test3 in Vivado can take up to five hours.
The generated build script ensures that the testbench and its dependencies (image and
result files) are added to the sim_1 fileset in Vivado, but it does not run the simulation
automatically.
Please note that in order to enable testing with Treadle and Verilator, two implemen-
tations of a clock gate are provided; one implementing the Xilinx BUFGCE primitive
[Sup18] and the other being the latch-based gate shown in figure 6.1, inspired by [Jon02].
The latch-based design consists of an active-low latch, a NAND and an inverter, which
together ensure that no input change while the clock is high causes an unwanted clock
pulse to be generated. This design assumes the gated clock is used only for positive-edge
triggered flip-flops. A synth parameter to the relevant Chisel modules selects between
the two such that they are used properly. This parameter also selects between two sets
of paths pointing to the memory initialization files of the evaluation memories such that
simulations work correctly both in Verilator and XSim.

D
Q
clk
gated clk
Figure 6.1: A latch-based clock gating cell, which ensures no glitches on the gated clock.
3The basic RTL behavioral test with the same source files in Vivado unfortunately fails with several
signals taking the undefined value X.
==PAGE:76==

6 Implementation
6.3.4
Continuous Integration
As mentioned previously, the source code is available open-source on GitHub.
The
repository is set up to run continuous integration (CI) checks on every pull request and
push to the master branch to ensure new additions do not break existing work. I decided
to use GitHub actions as my CI workflow as it is both easy to work with and directly
integrated into GitHub [Git].
To enable GitHub actions, the repository must contain a file .github/workflows/ci.yml,
which is a YAML format file describing step-by-step the pre-test setup and test execu-
tion of one or more test jobs. Currently, it describes two flows; unit tests and system
tests, respectively. The unit test flow performs the following steps in order:
1. Checkout the source code
2. Install Verilator, JDK 11, and Python 3.7
3. Install Python dependencies (PyTorch and BindsNET)
4. Compile the Scala code
5. Run the export.py script to export the pre-trained network to JSON
6. Run the Mapping.scala script to generate memory initialization files
7. Execute tests
while the system test flow performs the following steps, also in order:
1. Checkout the source code
2. Install Verilator and JDK 11
3. Compile the Scala code
4. Execute tests
The description of the unit test flow is shown in listing 6.2. In both flows, tests tagged
with SlowTest are ignored due to the monthly limit on CI minutes included in the free
version of GitHub4. Tests are run in an Ubuntu 20.04 environment to emulate that on
which the code was written. If all tests pass, a green badge shows up in the repository’s
readme section.

4Running the non-SlowTest tests takes roughly two minutes, while running the full test suite takes
roughly two hours.

==PAGE:78==

6 Implementation
6.4
Building the Project
To build the accelerator and run it on an FPGA board, first clone the git repository as:
git clone https://github.com/hansemandse/KWSonSNN.git
Next, follow the steps outlined in the project’s readme, which describe how to install
the required tools; Java JDK 8 (or 11), Scala build tool (SBT), Verilator, and Pip and
the Python libraries used. All other requirements should already be available on any
recent distribution of Ubuntu.
Now, simply run:
make all
Doing so builds the accelerator with SBT and outputs the Verilog code into a new
folder build, generates the memory mapping files as described in section 6.2, and removes
some `ifndef SYNTHESIS ... `endif wraps from the Verilog code ensuring correct
memory initialization. Finally, a shell script, mapping/gentcl.sh, generates a Tcl script
which can create a new project in Vivado, input all the source files, and start synthesis
and implementation runs up until the point of bit stream generation.
Subsequently, if Vivado is available in the command line, a new project may be setup
and initialized by running:
vivado -mode gui -source build.tcl
Alternatively, open a new Vivado window. Click Tools -> Run Tcl Script and
select the build script. Beware that the script assumes Vivado is installed in a Linux
environment and thus does not work on Windows.
The build targets the Digilent Genesys-2 board, which features a Xilinx Kintex-7
FPGA. To work properly on this board, which has a 200 MHz differential clock crystal,
the neuromorphic processor must be instantiated alongside an MMCM primitive, which
can divide the clock down to the targeted 80 MHz. This is done by using a Verilog
top-level file and an MMCM instantiation generated through Vivado’s clock wizard IP
generator tool. The top-level file is shown in listing 6.3. Subsequently, the top-level IO
is mapped to the relevant ports with the constraints file shown in listing 6.4.
Program the board by connecting its JTAG micro-USB port to the computer first,
and once programmed, switch to its UART port. Next, make sure to reset the accelerator
by pressing the button labelled BTNU. Finally, with the accelerator on the board, the
serial connection script netwkdev/test.py – available in appendix A listing A.9 – may
be used to run a series of test images on the accelerator and gather results, which are
presented in the form of a confusion matrix in the terminal.
==PAGE:79==
6.4 Building the Project

uartTx]

Listing 6.4: The top-level constraints for the Kintex-7 FPGA.
==PAGE:80==
66
6 Implementation
6.4.1
The SYNTHESIS Flag
Using loadMemoryFromFileInline in Chisel inserts readmemb or readmemh calls wrapped
inside `ifndef SYNTHESIS ... `endif in the generated Verilog code. This works fine
for simulation purposes, as the SYNTHESIS flag is not set in those cases, but according
to the IEEE Verilog RTL synthesis standard, any synthesis tool must implicitly set it,
see section 6.2 in [05]. This, however, leads to the initialization calls being removed in
preprocessing, if the wrap is not removed.
The alternative to using loadMemoryFromFileInline is to manually insert initializa-
tion calls into the generated Verilog code, as was done in [Rib20]. Unfortunately, doing
so makes testing with ChiselTest much more difficult, hence why the first option was
decided upon.
6.4.2
Chisel Top-level
As part of the final experiments of the project, a Chisel top-level module with corre-
sponding blackbox implementation of the MMCM was also produced. It is included in
appendix D listing D.4. Using a Chisel top module would mean fewer files are gener-
ated by building the accelerator. Unfortunately, a few experiments showed roughly 10%
higher resource consumption using the Chisel top module rather than the Verilog one
in listing 6.3. Despite double checking synthesis settings and constraints in Vivado, no
solution was found to this issue; hence why the Verilog top is still used.
Both the NeuroTop and ClkGen classes extend the Chisel RawModule class, which is
a simpler version of the Module class without implicit clock and reset. This was decided
upon to avoid such unused top-level signals, but should not affect Verilog generation. In
fact, apart from some signal names, the generated top-level Verilog is nearly identical
to the manually written code. Both versions run the provided testbench without errors.
==PAGE:81==
CHAPTER 7
Results
This chapter covers the results of the implemented design when synthesized and imple-
mented for a Xilinx Kintex-7 FPGA in Vivado. Additionally, the results are compared
to those presented in [Rib20]. Vivado is set up with the default synthesis and imple-
mentation strategies, retiming is disabled in both steps, flatten_hierarchy is also
disabled, but post-implementation and post-placement power optimization steps are en-
abled. Some of these results are further discussed in chapter 8.
7.1
Resource Consumption
Table 7.1 presents the resource consumption of the implemented accelerator with the
showcase network mapped to it on the aforementioned FPGA in a hierarchical structure.
These numbers must be seen in comparison to those in table 6.1 in [Rib20]. Accelerator
cores are numbered with their respective core IDs. The numbers result from the synthesis
step and show some interesting differences to the results of the baseline system. As
Vivado is configured to be allowed to rebuild the design architecture, synthesis results
are shown instead of implementation results. The rebuilding and placement optimization
steps might otherwise obscure the observations to be made from the resource utilization
due to seemingly random distribution of resources across otherwise unrelated modules.
The number of multiplexers implemented accounts only for the so-called F7 Muxes as
reported by Vivado; they act to combine the outputs of two 6-input LUTs either to select
between their functions or to enable mapping of 7-input Boolean equations directly to a
single logic slice. Implementation results show only a decrease in total number of LUTs
to 3899. All other measures are the same.
As in [Rib20], the overall resource consumption relative to the available resources of
the selected FPGA is quite low. The most noticeable differences are in the number of
BRAM cells and the number of FFs required. Both of these differences are explainable by
the evaluation memory optimizations introduced in section 5.4.2.2. Firstly, as predicted
during the design process, the overall number of BRAM cells per evaluation memory is
lower than before, and additional savings seem to be achieved by some of the four
memories not being mapped to BRAM cells but instead to registers.
Similarly, as
expected, both of the input cores and the output core are significantly smaller than
previously as they have had their memory-related hardware removed.
On the other
hand, the neuron cores have grown slightly in size as compared to the baseline system,
and they have slightly different sizes.

tioned synthesis settings.
To better understand the differences in resource consumption by the neuron cores,
consider table 7.2 which presents the resource consumption of neuron core 2. These
numbers must be seen in comparison to those in table 6.2 in [Rib20]. Clearly, as expected,
the increase in number of FFs consumed comes from the evaluation memories. More
surprising is the significantly increased number of LUTs consumed by the evaluation
units. Seeing as these are left mostly untouched (except for changing an 8:1 multiplexer
to a shift operation), this difference is inexplicable.
A synthesis run of the original
implementation of the neuron evaluator with the above synthesis settings showed a
resource consumption of 152 LUTs, i.e., more than consumption by the updated version
and much more than reported in [Rib20]. An additional comparison of their generated
Verilog codes also shows no major differences.
The size difference between the two
otherwise identical neuron cores arises from different sizes of the evaluation memories.
As two of the four memories in each evaluation memory module do not have write ports
and thus may be considered as ROMs, Vivado can apply different optimization strategies
to the differently initialized memories of each neuron core giving rise to these results.
Figure 7.1 shows the post-implementation floor plan of the accelerator mapped to the
FPGA. Each of the major modules is color coded. The schematic view clearly shows how
little of the available resources are consumed by the accelerator, and in comparison to
figure 6.1 in [Rib20], especially the lower number of BRAM cells used is clear. The cells
are concentrated in the lower half of the FPGA enabling the BRAM cells to be placed
near one another. All of the available clock buffers are located in the center of the FPGA.

Table 7.2: Post-synthesis resource consumption of neuron core 2 with the aforementioned
synthesis settings. Categories Eval. mems. and Eval. units sum together the eight of
each unit instantiated as part of the neuron core.
Thus, all global clocks are propagated from here via a central vertical clock backbone
and distributed to the individual clock regions via horizontal clock rows. Internal to
the clock regions, the clock is distributed based on a central clock management tile
[Sup18]. Neither table presented above includes clock buffers as these result in direct
instantiations of BUFGCTRL (a generalized BUFGCE) primitives with no other logic.
A more in-depth schematic view of the floor plan of neuron core 2 is shown in fig-
ure 7.2. It is interesting to see that the evaluation units are placed separately alongside
their evaluation memories, while the remainder of the core is somewhat linearly dis-
tributed in between the cores. This is likely done to improve timing and reduce total
wire length; two of the motivations behind the near-memory computing research direc-
tion which this accelerator finds inspiration from, as mentioned in chapter 4.
An interesting observation made during the process of generating these results is that
enabling flatten_hierarchy and setting it to rebuilt leads to a resource consumption
of a few hundred FFs fewer and more than a thousand more LUTs. This seems to be
due to certain components being able to share registers, while the neuron evaluators
require more LUTs. Similarly, to improve timing, the input memories are mapped to
pipelined LUT-RAM instead of BRAM cells. Due to these differences and to enable a
fair comparison with the results in [Rib20] – for which flatten_hierarchy was disabled
– the setting was left disabled.
==PAGE:84==

7 Results
Figure 7.1: Floor plan of the full accelerator. Purple: bus arbiter. Yellow: both input
memories. Green: both input cores. Blue: neuron core 2. Red: neuron core 3. Orange:
output core. Dark green: output FIFO. Light blue: off-chip communication.
7.2
System Timing
Despite not being in focus in this project, the results of the timing analysis in Vivado
are presented here. The system is still implemented with an 80 MHz clock frequency,
but with two different, but correlated clock domains. Post-implementation results are
used here to take place and route results into account.
The critical path of the circuit is within the core clock domain; specifically between
the axon system and one of the weight memories in neuron core 3. The path has a total
delay of 10.081 ns of which the logic delay is 3.272 ns and the remaining 6.809 ns are
net delay. This phenomenon of net delay dominating critical paths in FPGAs is not
uncommon due to their layout and the fact that they consist of many of the same logic
slices. It is particularly troublesome for BRAM-heavy systems such as this accelerator
because of the sparsity of BRAM cells. In this case, the net delay is large due to an
==PAGE:85==
7.2 System Timing
71
Figure 7.2: Floor plan of neuron core 2. Light blue: axon system. Orange: bus interface.
Pink: control unit. Blue: evaluation memories. Green: evaluation units. Red: spike
transmission. Cyan: the rest of the accelerator.
unfortunate mapping of the axon system to the bottom left corner of the FPGA, while
the evaluation memories are spread across the right half of the FPGA. Nonetheless,
the fact that the critical path has 2.419 ns negative slack suggests that Vivado has
not struggled to place and route the circuit. In comparison to the results presented in
[Rib20], this slack is significantly higher. This may likely also be explained by the fewer
BRAM cells leading to a less spread-out circuit with overall shorter nets.
Note that no attempts have been made to increase the clock frequency of the accel-
erator. Seeing as it targets low-power applications, its operating frequency should be
kept as low as possible; naturally, taking evaluation time into account ensuring that all
neurons can be evaluated in each time step under worst-case spike activity. Nonethe-
less, the results presented above indicate that the accelerator is capable of running at
significantly higher frequencies than targeted, should that be wanted.
==PAGE:86==
72
7 Results
7.3
Power Consumption
As described in chapter 5, the optimizations introduced to the accelerator have primarily
focused on reducing its power consumption. As such, the results presented here are of
utmost importance to prove the benefits of the changes made. The results are gathered
in a similar way as done in [Rib20] using the testbench shown in appendix C listing C.22
to generate a switching activity interchange format (SAIF) file. The testbench tests a
full inference run on the accelerator starting out by transmitting an indirectly rank-order
encoded input image – in this case, a random utterance of the keyword ”up” – and next
waiting for 500 time steps while collecting and comparing the generated spikes to a set
of expected spikes generated by the corresponding ChiselTest tester.
As such, the simulation time spans roughly 669 ms. The produced SAIF file may be
used to generate a post-implementation power report whose findings are summarized in
the overview shown in figure 7.3. These results must be seen in comparison to those of
figure 6.3 in [Rib20] and show significant savings. Firstly, the total estimated power is
322 mW consisting of 150 mW dynamic power and 172 mW static power. Compared to
the baseline’s 989 mW, 539 mW and 450 mW, this corresponds to reductions of 67%,
72% and 62%, respectively. Comparing more in detail, the reduction in dynamic power
seems to be driven by two factors; the overall reduction in number of BRAMs and the
different spike encoding giving rise to significantly less activity in the accelerator – in
fact, the reduction in BRAM dynamic power is nearly 99%. Similarly, the main driving
factor of the reduction in static power must the overall smaller area of the accelerator.
As in [Rib20], it is positive to see that signals and logic each consume less than 1 mW of
dynamic power. This means that the attempts at implementing efficient hardware have
been successful.
Figure 7.3: An overview of the power estimate produced by Vivado when supplying
it with the SAIF file from the aforementioned simulation.
Notice in particular the
significantly lower dynamic power consumption in BRAMs.
==PAGE:87==
7.3 Power Consumption
73
The spike activity is interesting to comment on further. Due to the configuration
of the excitatory layer Ae and the inhibitory layer Ai of the showcase network, a single
spike from layer Ae gives rise to 199 spikes produced by layer Ai. Using the network
provided in [Rib20] with rate encoding and greater neuron thresholds than here, an input
image may give rise to more than 1000 input spikes resulting in roughly 100-150 spikes
produced by layer Ae. With higher thresholds, many input spikes are required to cause
a neuron in layer Ae to spike, so this relation makes sense but implies many BRAM
accesses the neuron cores. Meanwhile, with lower thresholds and rank-order encoded
inputs as used here, the test image gives rise to just 23 spikes from layer Ae.
To better understand where the power is consumed, consider table 7.3 which presents
the dynamic power consumption split across individual modules using the same hierarchi-
cal structure as in table 7.1. As indicated in the table, these results suffer from Vivado’s
limited accuracy but still show a clear picture. The MMCM takes up the majority of
the dynamic power consumption (86% to be precise) and roughly 40% of the total power
consumption. Otherwise, the remaining dynamic power seems to be burnt in the clock
network. Comparing to [Rib20], the clock power has actually increased by 2 mW, which
might be the result of the introduced BUFGCEs. This is rather unexpected as a major
part of the clock network is gated whenever the accelerator is idle. More on this in
section 8.2.

Despite the poor performance of the showcase network presented in table 3.2, there
is no reason to expect greater spike activity if a better performing network of the same
size is mapped to the accelerator. This is due to the normalization of weights in the
training script that ensures the sum of weights within a single neuron’s receptive field
does not exceed a set value, and that the input layer is insensitive to differences in input
image intensity as each pixel can at most lead to one input spike.
7.4
Classification Accuracy
Finally, the classification accuracy of the showcase network mapped to the accelerator
must be tested to better understand the effects of the quantization introduced in sec-
tion 5.4.2.2. These results are gathered by running inferences directly on the accelerator
with the test script given in appendix A listing A.9. The resulting confusion matrix is
shown in table 7.5.
The results resemble the seemingly random classification behavior of the software
model of the network. This makes it difficult to conclude anything regarding the impact
of the weight quantization introduced. The intuition is that a network that does not
initially have a reasonably high classification accuracy meaning that the receptive field
of each neuron is not well-tuned into a specific class, will experience a greater impact on
its already poor accuracy when quantized. This seems to be the case for the showcase
network here.

However, seeing as the neuron evaluation components of the accelerator remain

==PAGE:91==
CHAPTER 8
Discussion
This chapter covers discussions of the classification accuracy of the showcase network
both in terms of sources to this inaccuracy and potential solutions to it. Next, the
power consumption reductions are briefly discussed. Then follows an extensive list of
future work is covered. The future work focuses primarily on hardware redesign of the
accelerator, and a little on the flow from SNN model in Python to a Chisel description
of a corresponding accelerator.
Finally, the discussion briefly mentions how further
optimizations may be achieved when targeting an ASIC implementation, and what to
expect in terms of power consumption in such an environment.
8.1
Classification Accuracy
From the results presented in chapter 3 section 3.2.3, it is clear that the showcase network
does not perform as well as should be expected. As argued previously, this is likely due
to the network being too simple and/or the training algorithms of BindsNET being
unable to capture the hard-to-predict spike patterns of GSCD.
8.1.1
Sources of Inaccuracy
Judging by table 3.2, in particular the ”down”, ”no”, ”go”, and ”unknown” classes are
predicted almost randomly among the other classes. ”up” and ”off” are particularly
popular predictions, which may be explained by their short pronunciations leading to
few, high-valued weights in their respective receptive fields. The high-valued weights will,
due to the centering of audio snippets and relatively similar MFCs across all classes, lead
to many predictions of those two classes1. This is unfortunately unavoidable as it is a
result of how the network is trained.
An interesting observation from the numerous training runs carried out as part of
this project is that a specific network configuration may provide reasonable (i.e., better
than as reported here) classification accuracy when training the network for only a
few epochs with significantly fewer examples, while training a network with the exact
same parameters with all of the examples will end up performing worse. Similarly, first-
epoch results tend to be better than results arising from training for more epochs. This
phenomenon seems to be insensitive to hyper parameters such as, e.g., learning rates
1A similar trend is seen with the ”1” class of MNIST which also has few high-valued weights in
similar positions to those of ”4”, ”7”, and ”9”.
==PAGE:92==
78
8 Discussion
and normalization factors. Hence, this underlines the fact that the network is incapable
of generalizing well enough to capture the patterns of GSCD.
8.1.2
Potential Solutions
This poor performance is, of course, not satisfactory and renders the trained network
rather unusable in practice. Arguably, the classification task would become easier if no
two keywords have (partially) similar pronunciation. Using this logic, it would make
sense to avoid pairs such as ”on” and ”off”, and ”no” and ”go”. Unfortunately, many
applications of KWS require natural combinations of words with similar pronunciations,
and thus the network should perform well even under this requirement. Instead, to
achiever better classification accuracy, I suggest following two paths:
• explore different MFC resolutions
• explore alternative network architectures
Despite the preprocessing steps presumably being correct, the MFC resolution is still
a potential path to explore for better classification accuracy. The current dataset imple-
mentation is configured to use a frame length and a number of filters ensuring square
MFCs, although this might not be the optimal configuration; in fact, some related work
uses a longer frame length and fewer filters [Wan+20]. Exploring different configurations
is, thus, interesting. Additionally, just like MNIST images traditionally are 28x28 pix-
els, perhaps using higher resolution MFCs with either more filters or more frames evenly
distributed along the entire second of the GSCD snippets will provide better results; for
example, 49x40 MFCs are used in [Zha+18]. Increasing MFC resolution does, however,
increase the size of the input layer of the network, the number of weights, and thus the
memory consumption of the accelerator. Hence, this is likely an accuracy-area tradeoff.
8.1.3
Architectural Considerations
Most modern image classification tasks employ more advanced network architectures
than simple single-layer FFNNs. Deeper networks with more weights are often capable
of capturing different patterns in each layer to gain better knowledge on each input
and thus provide higher overall classification accuracy. However, in the SNN-related
literature, it is unclear exactly how a multi-layer SNN would be constructed. Initial
experiments with introduction of more excitatory layers without lateral inhibition (i.e.,
one excitatory neuron spiking leads to inhibition of all other excitatory neurons in a
layer) in the showcase network did not provide any better results than what was achieved
with the showcase network. It is likely that if networks have layers that are primarily
excitatory, they should each have a corresponding inhibitory layer to reduce their activity.
An example of such a configuration is shown in figure 8.1. Naturally, this means selection
of even more network parameters and thus a potentially significantly more difficult
==PAGE:93==
8.1 Classification Accuracy
79
optimization process. It also means many more weights and thus a significantly larger
accelerator area.
Input layer
Output layer
Hidden layer
Hidden layer
exc.
inh.
exc.
inh.
exc.
inh.
train.
train.
train.
Figure 8.1: A deeper version of the showcase network in which all layers in the forward
path have lateral inhibition.
Alternatively, the network architecture could be drastically changed into for example
a convolutional model. Convolutional neural networks (CNNs) are networks which con-
tain layers that convolve weighted filters with input feature maps to extract higher-order
features. Switching to such an architecture will likely enable the network to recognize
class-specific patterns in the preprocessed MFCs that the current FFNN does not. Addi-
tionally, adding first a convolutional layer and next a pooling layer will reduce the size of
the resulting image and thus the size of the remaining layers of the network. Overall, this
means that the memory consumption of the accelerator might not increase, although its
area likely will due to the addition of extra hardware for the convolutions. Alternatively,
if the accelerator is to operate all the time, it might make sense to explore network archi-
tectures that support streaming in inputs. Recurrent neural networks (RNNs), or even
recurrent convolutional neural networks (RCNNs), are interesting alternatives. Both re-
current network types require introduction of more weights and more neuron values that
need storage. This means increases in both storage and area for updating the recurrent
state. All three architectures have spiking equivalents, although they seem to appear in
fewer articles than regular SNNs [RV20; VCS19]2.
Figure 8.2a shows a spiking version of a 2D convolutional connection with a single
input feature map and a single 3x3 filter. Note that the input feature map is updated in
each time step and consists only of binary entries making the indicated multiplications
simple AND-operations. Figure 8.2b shows a spiking long short-term memory (LSTM)
cell proposed in [RV20], with its characteristic three ”gates”; the forget gate to clear the
cell’s content, the input gate taking in new inputs, and the output gate which calculates
its output based on both current input and stored history. LSTM cells require a lot of
storage, but few cells can provide good performance in sequence tasks.
Searching for the optimal network configuration for a specific dataset is a tedious
process and one that requires a more in-depth understanding of the nature of spiking
neural networks than what has been part of this project. From the literature, it seems
2Likely due to deep SNN and recurrent SNN research being in their infancy, as pointed out in [RV20]
==PAGE:94==
80
8 Discussion
