Figure 8.2: Example cells for spiking CNN and LSTM models.
networks are designed for specific use cases, and they often employ features that are not
available in the computing-oriented SNN libraries such as BindsNET. For example, the
adapted showcase network used in this project is a less-advanced version of a network
targeting the MNIST dataset as presented in [DC15], in which the authors use a different
library with more biologically plausible synapse models and different STDP algorithms.
It is worth reconsidering whether BindsNET is the right library to use for this pur-
pose. While it does provide 2D convolutional and max-pooling connections, see here,
it unfortunately does not have any recurrent topologies, nor does it support more ad-
vanced synapse models. A potential alternative is the Nengo library meant for Intel’s
Loihi, which supports both layer types and is likely the most well-developed and most
used SNN library [Bek+14]. Nengo bases itself directly on Numpy rather than an exist-
ing ANN development library, which means that it provides a more clean development
flow as it does not inherit anything unrelated. Alternatively, one might explore the
ANN-to-SNN conversion strategy of training a suitable ANN model and utilizing, e.g.,
SNN Toolbox for converting it into a corresponding SNN [Rue+17]. This has proven to
be possible with little accuracy reduction.
8.2
Power Consumption
The results presented in the previous chapter are clear in that they show a significant
power reduction as compared to the original accelerator of [Rib20]. However, the reduc-
tion seems to arise almost solely from memory and spike encoding-related optimizations,
and not from the implemented clock gating – which actually seems to slightly increase
the clock network power consumption. The increase might be explained by the introduc-
==PAGE:95==
8.3 Future Work
81
tion of two extra controllable clock buffers, or it might be a result of different routing.
Some effects are masked by Vivado rounding all power measures to three decimals.
The initial expectation was for clock gating to bring relatively large savings due to
reduced switching the accelerator cores. This expectation was reinforced by an experi-
ment carried out in the VLSI design course at DTU, where a comparison of three shift
register implementations – an always-active register, a clock enable based register, and
a clock gating-based register – showed nearly 50% power reductions from clock gating
and only 10% with clock enable. Furthermore, clock gating is listed as future work in
[Rib20]. However a more in-depth look at the source code for the baseline system after
the project start revealed that the system employs clock enables on almost all of the
registers which see the most activity, and all its FSMs have an idle state that they wait
in once they finish their execution till a new time step beings. All memories are also
disabled when not needed. As a result of this, introducing clock gating at a high level
did not provide nearly the expected effects.
Naturally, FPGAs provide only a good platform for testing different digital systems,
but their architecture means that power estimates tend to be significantly higher than
what may be expected from an equivalent ASIC implementation. More on this in sec-
tion 8.3.6.
8.3
Future Work
This section presents a handful of further optimization options of the accelerator as well
as related work that is worth investigating further but could not be fit into the limited
time of the project.
8.3.1
Neuron Core
The neuron cores did not receive as much attention in this project as expected. They
should be the main focus for optimizations as they include the most hardware and
perform the most work in the accelerator. As such, a few possible optimizations are
presented here.
While relatively flexible, the current structure of the neuron cores is complex and
would thus benefit from having its control system be more closely linked to the eval-
uation memories and neuron evaluators. As described in section 5.3.3.3, the meuron
evaluation process is still memory bound being limited by the shared output interface
of the evaluation memory module connected to the neuron evaluators. Implementing
instead a combined neuron evaluation unit with split memory and evaluation hardware
would enable reduction of the number of states of the control unit FSM significantly as
multiple unrelated values could be read concurrently. This would reduce the number of
cycles required for updating all neurons in a layer and thus enable either mapping more
neurons to a core or hardware savings from fewer evaluation units being required for the
current number of neurons. It might be that a different mapping of network parameters
==PAGE:96==
82
8 Discussion
to the individual memory modules that presented in section 5.4.2.2 would be beneficial
for this purpose; a limitation of the current implementation is that refractory counters
and membrane potentials cannot be read/written in the same cycle. Grouping together
instead membrane potentials and thresholds, and refractory counters and biases would
likely enable fewer states in the control unit FSM.
Another change that would decrease the precision gap between software model and
the accelerator is the introduction of a reset loop at the end of an inference. In the train-
ing scripts, this is managed by calling BindsNET’s reset_state_variables method,
which resets both refractory counters and membrane potentials of all neurons to their
specified reset values. In the case of the accelerator, the introduction of this loop would
require adding a couple of new states and a time step counter to the neuron cores’ control
FSM, as well as introducing an extra time step delay between two consecutive inference
runs; something that could be managed by slightly altering the phase system used to
indicate readiness between the accelerator cores and the off-chip communication.
8.3.2
Communication Fabric
As shown in figure 4.1a, the accelerator cores should communicate over any generic AER-
based communication fabric. In the current implementation, the accelerator is rather
locked to using a basic bus due to the incorporation of bus interfaces and spike transmis-
sion systems directly in the cores. Combined with the internal AER of coreID # neuronID
which limits the number of neurons which can be mapped to each core to 256, this is
a rather unflexible way to connect cores, and also implies a need for two input cores to
implement even the simple showcase network.
Intuitively, one would expect an accelerator of this kind to be so generic that it
implements only one input core, one output core, and any number of neuron cores. This
may be achieved with the following two changes:
• A generalized communication fabric
• An alternative AER
The communication fabric should be viewed as any n-port ”cloud” to which the accel-
erator cores connect. The connection may be made using regular ready-valid interfaces,
as shown in figure 8.3. Using such generic interfaces to the cores, the ”cloud” should
be interchangeable depending on the number of cores and expected spike activity. This
means that a bus may still be used if the size of the accelerator allows it, while larger ac-
celerators can employ, e.g., any broadcast-capable NoC architecture [Fan+18; Dav+18].
Similarly, the AER may be altered slightly to layerID # neuronID such that it is not
only more intuitive for the engineer to work with but also no longer poses an obstacle
in terms of the number of neurons that may be mapped to a specific core. In fact, with
this AER, it should be possible to map more than one layer to a particular core.
Introducing these changes, however, mean a significant redesign of the accelerator is
required, as it affects not only the mapping of layers to cores, the axon system, spike
==PAGE:97==
8.3 Future Work
83
valid
data
ready
valid
data
ready
Accelerator core
Arbiter
RV-based
bus interf.
RV-based
bus interf.
RV-based
NI
Figure 8.3: A generic communication fabric with corresponding ready-valid interfaces
on all cores. In this illustration, two fabrics are pictured; a bus and a torus NoC.
transmission and input and output cores. It is unlikely that this change will lead to lower
power or resource consumption, but it will make for a much more generic accelerator.
8.3.3
Time Step Flexibility
The design is currently configured such that there are neuron evaluators enough to
evaluate spikes from all axons to all neurons correctly within a single time step. However,
as proven in [Rib20], the accelerator is inactive in almost 99% of the time in each time
step. This introduces a trade-off between the accelerator area in terms of how many
evaluation units are implemented, and how many spikes can be treated in each time
step.
Reducing the number of evaluation units means that the accelerator might experience
the rare case of there being too many spikes to correctly update all neurons within a
time step. In this case, the accelerator may employ a flexible time step length such that
it is possible for one (or a few) time step(s) to borrow from the subsequent time step(s),
which then become relatively shorter. Combining this feature with a communication
fabric and axon system which can better exploit the sparse spike activity can potentially
lead to power savings.
Currently, as described in section 5.3.3.5, the top-level neuromorphic processor mod-
ule keeps track of time steps with a cycle counter and indicates to the cores when a
new time step begins. This control could be made more flexible by replacing the sim-
ple counter with a combined state machine and counter controlled by the same signals
currently used to control clock gating. This would introduce little extra logic in the top-
==PAGE:98==
84
8 Discussion
level while allowing reduction of the number of evaluation units to be just one in each
neuron core. The axon system could, because of its time-multiplexed nature, be made
completely asynchronous by introducing local clock generation similar to the methods
shown in [Wan+20].
The flexibility could be taken to the extreme by switching to fully asynchronous
evaluation, such that each spike is used for updates when received. This is done for
example in [Dav+18], but would require a significant redesign of the entire accelerator
as well as a switch away from using Chisel.
8.3.4
Further Memory Optimizations
Despite some area savings achieved by altering the quantization strategy presented in
this project, further optimizations are likely achievable. Most noticeably, techniques
such as memory compression and network pruning can reduce the memory requirements
of most networks significantly; [HMD16] reports 35x to 49x reductions in size for state-
of-the-art ANN models, and, using an alternative training algorithm than the previously
mentioned STDP-based ones, their techniques are suitable for SNNs too [Den+20].
Network pruning is the act of removing certain low-weight connections from the
network during training. Pruning in itself can reduce model size by up to 10 times
with little effect on the accuracy, when the model is retrained with pruning. Similarly,
applying various compression algorithms (traditionally, run-length or Huffman coding)
may be applied to the pruned weights to achieve greater reductions. This is due to the
observed great similarity between trained weights [HMD16].
The accelerator would, of course, require some additional hardware to implement
both pruned and compressed networks as both would (potentially) affect the evaluation
memories and their addressing modes as well as output hardware; especially for handling
compression algorithms efficiently, as run-length coding requires a linear-time algorithm
to decompress it, while Huffman coding requires a dictionary lookup [Ltd].
Additional power savings may be achievable by introducing a different memory layout
of the evaluation memories. Like caches, arranging the memories in blocks of multiple
weights (e.g., one or a power of two block(s) per neuron) and reading out an entire block
into a register buffer would reduce the number of accesses made to the memory itself.
Assuming more than a certain percentage of the weights in a block are used, this might
provide some savings. If not, it is likely that the overhead of reading out many more
bits at once may be less efficient than the current implementation. Nevertheless, this is
worth exploring.
8.3.5
Reconfigurability
To function in a real product, the accelerator needs to be reconfigurable on-the-fly to
enable efficient changes between different network models or simply different network
weights. Further reconfiguration options include support for different spike encodings in
the input cores and different neuron models in the neuron cores.
==PAGE:99==
8.3 Future Work
85
Currently, supporting reconfiguration of the network weights would be a relatively
little change of adding a second port to the evaluation memories to be able to overwrite
them from an external control unit. However, the network model is hardwired by the
ROMs included in the bus interfaces; but replacing the ROMs with equivalently sized
RAMs again with outward-facing write ports controlled by an external unit would allow
changing the network model on-the-fly.
Nonetheless, such introductions also require
changes to the off-chip communication module such that it may differ between incom-
ing spike periods and reconfiguration parameters. With the data amounts required for
representing even small networks like the showcase network used in this project, the
UART-based communication becomes impractical.
Instead, the accelerator could be
(and likely would be) placed in a system alongside a microcontroller capable of repro-
gramming it much faster.
Supporting different spike encodings could be enabled by implementing all the dif-
ferent encodings and selecting between them with a multiplexer controlled by a register.
Currently, two spike encodings are available in the source code – rate and rank-order –
but only one is implemented, selected by a parameter RANKORDERENC given in the pack-
age description. The register could be written to by the same external control circuit
that controls the other memories. An illustration of this kind of system is provided in
figure 8.4.

Figure 8.4: System to switch between spike encodings.
A similar strategy can be applied for supporting different neuron models, except such
an addition will have more wide-spread effects on both neuron evaluators, evaluation
memories, and the neuron core control units. The evaluators could be extended with
controllability in a similar way to that for supporting different spike encodings, while
the evaluation memories could be made sufficiently large such that they can contain all
of the required neuron parameters regardless of neuron model. Control units could be
extended with states for evaluating all possible neuron models and simply configured
to take different paths through the state graph depending on the neuron model in use.
However, this would require a major redesign of the accelerator.
Since the accelerator modules are all parameterizable, the ideal work flow would be
to define an SNN model in a Python library such as BindsNET and be able to export a
corresponding accelerator implementation with core mappings and weight initialization
directly. This flow would benefit from the aforementioned updates to the AER and
==PAGE:100==
86
8 Discussion
communication fabric as well as memory optimizations presented before, as the mapping
could focus solely on the neuron cores and explore which compression options work the
best. Furthermore, introducing different neuron models and spike encodings and being
able to select between them would make for a highly flexibly library of modules, and
one that could be used to explore different accelerator architectures very quickly. The
flow is illustrated in figure 8.5.

Despite not being shown in the flow, it is assumed that the generated accelerator
supports the reconfiguration options described above such that the initialization only
provides a starting point as part of the bitstream.
8.3.6
ASIC Implementation
A final task that still needs exploration is an ASIC implementation of the accelerator in
a representative process. At the time of writing, students at DTU can only access a basic
suite of Synopsys tools including Design Vision for synthesis and VCS for simulation.
However, only a custom – rather simple – 90 nm process used in the VLSI design course
is available, and Design Vision does not include a memory compiler. Thus, to generate
reasonable results, a greater tool suite is required.
It must be expected that the power results presented in section 7.3 are significantly
higher than what can be expected in an equivalent ASIC implementation. [KR07] re-
ports that, on average, one can expect up to 14x lower power consumption, depending
on the mixture of logic, memory and arithmetic cells used in the design.
However,
the authors of [KR07] do not take clock gating into account. It is likely that exactly
clock gating has a greater effect on dynamic power in an ASIC environment, where
==PAGE:101==
8.3 Future Work
87
the clock network is a more significant part of the total dynamic power consumption,
but this is hard to judge from FPGA power numbers. Replacing existing clock enables
with lower-level clock gating at individual modules or registers might lead to greater
reductions, but this introduces extra hardware for generating the clock gate signals that,
because of the already low switching activity in the accelerator, might limit the overall
effects on power savings. Furthermore, an ASIC implementation can utilize more recent
advances in low-power memories to implement more power-efficient non-volatile evalu-
ation memories. Using non-volative memory technologies allows for implementation of
power-gating to further reduce static power consumption; optimizations not available in
an FPGA context. Technologies such as resistive RAM (RRAM) and magnetoresistive
RAM (MRAM) can provide good trade-offs between power consumption and delay, and
they are non-volatile [Rze+21; Raj+19].
With the same assumptions as in [Rib20] section 7.1.1, based on [KR07], it is possible
to provide a rough estimate for the power of an equivalent ASIC implementation based
on the FPGA results presented in table 7.3. As such, the system is considered in its
current form without the MMCM, and operating under the expected temperature of
a hearing aid.
With these assumptions, [Rib20] derives a 7x expected reduction in
dynamic power and a 70x reduction in static power leading to a power consumption
of 6.31 mW as per equation 8.1. This number is, again, significantly lower than the
65.9 mW reported in [Rib20] and thus brings the accelerator much closer to the expected
sub-5 mW operating power of an accelerator for a hearing aid. With the optimizations
described in this chapter implemented, this power consumption may be reduced further.
This estimate must be considered very rough. A more accurate estimate
150 mW −123 mW
7
+ 172 mW

(8.1)
Seeing as the accelerator remains capable of performing 263 million synaptic opera-
tions per second [Rib20], its power efficiency with the above power estimate is now 41.7
million synaptic operations per mW. This brings it within a factor of 16 of the efficiency
of IBM’s TrueNorth chip [Ako+15], albeit with much less functionality. If implemented
with an ASIC flow, the results in terms of area and power would need to be compared
to other more similar designs or to the other modules of the overall system in which the
accelerator is to be placed, all implemented in the same process. This might be rather
difficult due to limited publicity of such systems.
==PAGE:102==
88
==PAGE:103==
CHAPTER 9
Conclusion
This thesis has documented the work carried out as part of the project of optimizing
a hardware accelerator for spiking neural networks and converting it from executing
simple digit recognition to a KWS application. The chapters presented basic background
information about SNNs and the Chisel HDL, the network development and training,
the baseline system and the optimizations introduced. This was followed by a short
description of relevant implementation details, and the results obtained.
Finally, a
discussion presented reasons as to why the showcase network performs rather poorly
along with potential solutions to this issue, a short evaluation of the power consumption
results achieved, as well as potential future work that did not fit into the project period.
SNNs are attractive alternatives to regular multiplication-based ANNs due in partic-
ular to their sparse activity and addition-based neuron updates. Their architecture and
operations map particularly well to application-specific near-memory computing acceler-
ators. However, as computing-oriented SNNs is a relatively young area of research, the
libraries used to define and train such models are under-developed and harder to use com-
pared to their ANN counterparts. Nonetheless, the project presents an implementation
of a showcase FFNN in the BindsNET library, which achieves only 31.5% classification
accuracy on the preprocessed GSCD, and a corresponding hardware accelerator to which
the showcase network maps.
The accelerator bases itself around a number of cores; two input cores, a parame-
terizable number of neuron cores and one output core, interconnected by a common
AER-based bus. Input images are fed to the acceleration in an indirect period-based
rank-order encoding and output spikes are read from the accelerator, both via a UART
connection. High-level clock gating is applied such that the accelerator cores are all
switched off by a shared clock buffer when they are finished evaluating and waiting for
the next time step to begin. Network parameters are mapped to updated evaluation
memory modules which target a 7.10 fixed-point format, but include weights in two
other formats, 6.4 and 0.10, which are converted on-the-fly.
The hardware results show that the optimized accelerator uses roughly the same
number of LUTs, much fewer BRAMs, and slightly more FFs than the baseline design.
This, combined with the reduction in spike activity from using rank-order encoding,
leads to significant power savings. The full system consumes a total of 322 mW, just
32.5% of the baseline design’s total power consumption. This was found to be the result
of a 99% reduction of BRAM dynamic power and a 61.7% reduction of static power.
==PAGE:104==
90
9 Conclusion
The discussion starts with a presentation of some sources to the showcase network’s
inaccuracy as well as potential solutions to these. The conclusion to this is that the
network has too few trainable weights to learn the more-difficult patterns of preprocessed
GSCD and that potential solutions include different preprocessing steps and changes to
the network architecture and size. Finally, it continues by presenting considerations
on future work. The conclusion to this is that most of the accelerator modules should
be redesigned in a reconfigurable, modular fashion based around a generic AER-based
interconnect.
Overall, the project provides answers to the questions set out in the project descrip-
tion and presents an accelerator which is much closer to the targeted sub-5 mW ultra
low-power hearing aid application than the baseline.

54

56

100
A Network Development

63

66

67

'all given keywords must be part of the dataset'

def __getitem__(self, ind):
109
"""
110
Generates slightly randomized shifts and noise versions of
the audio signals.
111
"""
112
if not self.processed:
113
audio = self.audio[ind][:self.num_samples]
114
else:
115
audio = self.audio[ind]
116
117
label = self.labels[ind]
118
return {'audio': audio, 'label': label}

def _get_train(self) -> Tuple[List[torch.Tensor], torch.Tensor,

131

remove_files))

os.path.join(root, x), filenames)))

# Fix difference between Win32 and Unix paths
161

files))

165

167
print(f"Fetching keyword '{kw}'", flush=True)
168
audio, labels , sr = self._fetch_data(files)

torch.save(

(audio, labels , sr),

def _get_unknowns(self, pf: str, audiolen: int) ->
Tuple[List[np.ndarray], List[str], int]:
308
"""
309
Get a set of unknown keywords randomly sampled from unused
keywords.
310
311
:param pf: The pickle file ending to use.
312
:param audiolen: The length of the current audio array.
313
:return: A tuple of randomly sampled unused keywords.
314
"""
315
# Check if this combination of unknowns have been generated
before
316
ukhash =
hashlib.sha1('_'.join(self.unknowns).encode('utf-8')).hexdigest()
317
ukpath = os.path.join(
318
self.path, SpeechCommandsDataset.unknown_folder , ukhash
+ pf
319
)
320
if os.path.isfile(ukpath):
321
u_audio , u_labels , u_sr = torch.load(open(ukpath, 'rb'))
322
# ... otherwise , generate it and store it
323
else:
324
u_audio , u_labels , u_sr = [], [], -1
325
per_class = int((audiolen / len(self.kws)) /
len(self.unknowns))
326
327
# Run through all unknown keywords
328
for kw in self.unknowns:
329

def _fetch_data(
452
self, files: Iterable[str]
453
) -> Tuple[List[np.ndarray], np.ndarray , int]:
454
"""
455
Opens files of Speech Commands data and processes them into
``numpy`` arrays.
456
Labels are one-hot encoded according to their position in
``self.kws``.
==PAGE:124==
110
A Network Development
457
458
:param files: Names of the files containing Spoken MNIST
audio to load.
459
:return: Processed Speech Commands audio and label data.
460
"""
461
audio, labels = [], []
462
exp_sr , _ = wavfile.read(files[0])
463
464
# For subsampling - to center the audio, the subsampled
frames are weighted
465
# by a window giving central samples greater weight
466
frame_length , frame_stride = 8000, 1000
467
num_frames = (exp_sr - frame_length) // frame_stride + 1
468
indices = (
469
np.tile(np.arange(0, frame_length), (num_frames , 1))
470
+ np.tile(
471
np.arange(0, num_frames * frame_stride ,
frame_stride), (frame_length , 1)
472
).T
473
)
474
weight = np.tile(np.blackman(frame_length), (num_frames , 1))
475
476
# For each file, fetch the audio signal
477
pbar = tqdm(files)
478
for f in pbar:
479
# Get label from file name
480
label = f.split('/')[-2]
481
482
# Get signal from .wav file
483
sr, signal = wavfile.read(f)
484
if sr != exp_sr:
485
raise ValueError("all audio files must have the
same sampling rate")
486
487
# Taken directly from
488
#
https://github.com/BindsNET/bindsnet/blob/master/bindsnet/datasets/
489
# also inspired by
490
#
https://towardsdatascience.com/speech-classification -using-neural-n
491
pre_emphasis = 0.97
492
signal = np.append(
493
signal[0], signal[1:] - pre_emphasis * signal[:-1]
494
)
495
496
# Pad to length of 1 second
==PAGE:125==
A.2 The kwsonsnn Library
111
497
signal = np.hstack(
498
(signal ,
np.random.normal(scale=abs(np.median(signal)),
size=exp_sr -len(signal)))
499
)
500
501
# Pick out sub-part of the signal - first attempt uses
8000 samples (i.e., half)
502
frames = signal[indices.astype(np.int32, copy=False)]
503
signal = frames[np.argmax(np.sum(np.dot(np.abs(frames),
weight.T), 1))]
504
505
# Normalization for easier plotting - add constant to
avoid true_divide error
506
signal /= max(np.abs(signal)) + 1e-9
507
508
# Append the signal and its label to the lists
509
with warnings.catch_warnings():
510
warnings.simplefilter("ignore")
511
audio.append(signal), labels.append(label)
512
513
return audio, np.array(labels), exp_sr

def _process_data(self) -> None:
516
"""
517
Applies common FFT-related techniques to the audio signals
but leaves
518
the labels. Must be called explicitly to affect the
dataset. Non-reversible.
519
"""
520
if self.processed:
521
print('Dataset has already been processed!')
522
return
523
self.processed = True
524
525
# Run through all audio and labels
526
audio, labels = [], []
527
pbar = tqdm(zip(self.audio, self.labels))
528
for signal , label in pbar:
529
# Again, taken more or less directly from
530
#
https://github.com/BindsNET/bindsnet/blob/master/bindsnet/datas
531
532
# Various sources use different frame sizes - typical
values in [10ms, 40ms]
533
# Similarly , different strides (and hence, overlaps)
==PAGE:126==
112
A Network Development
are used - typical values in [0%, 75%]
534
# For example ,
https://github.com/BindsNET/bindsnet/blob/master/bindsnet/datasets/
535
# uses frame_size=25ms and frame_stride=10ms (60%
overlap)
536
# whereas Hello Edge (https://arxiv.org/abs/1711.07128)
537
# uses frame_size=40ms and frame_stride=20ms (50%
overlap)
538
# and Benchmarking KWS Efficiency ...
(https://arxiv.org/abs/1812.01739)
539
# uses frame_size=.... and frame_stride=10ms (...
overlap).
540
# I choose to use the same setup as Hello Edge
targeting a smaller network as presented
541
# in Benchmarking KWS Efficiency (i.e., no
convolutions).
542
543
# Also, the Mel Spectral Frequency Component analysis
transforms the inputs to
544
# the same size as the images used as part of the
previous work by Anthon (i.e., 22x22).
545
# TODO: Make this code more flexible - for example
allowing selection of frame_length.
546
547
# In seconds ... (like Hello Edge now)
548
frame_length = 0.04
549
frame_stride = 0.02
550
551
assert frame_length <= 1.0, (
552
f'frames should be shorter than or equal to signal
length , got {frame_length}'
553
)
554
assert frame_stride < frame_length , (
555
f'frame stride should be less than frame length ,
got {frame_length} and {frame_stride}'
556
)
557
558
# ... converted to samples
559
frame_length , frame_stride = (
560
int(round(frame_length * self.sr)),
561
int(round(frame_stride * self.sr))
562
)
563
signal_length = len(signal)
564
565
# Pad to ensure at least one frame
566
num_frames = int(np.ceil(float(np.abs(signal_length -
==PAGE:127==
A.2 The kwsonsnn Library
113
frame_length)) / frame_stride))
567
pad_signal_length = num_frames * frame_stride +
frame_length
568
signal = np.append(signal, np.zeros((pad_signal_length
- signal_length)))
569
570
# Collect frames from signal
571
indices = (
572
np.tile(np.arange(0, frame_length), (num_frames , 1))
573
+ np.tile(
574
np.arange(0, num_frames * frame_stride ,
frame_stride), (frame_length , 1)
575
).T
576
)
577
frames = signal[indices.astype(np.int32, copy=False)]
578
579
# A Hamming window is used to reduce noise in the FFT
due to cuts in the signal
580
frames *= np.hamming(frame_length)
581
582
# Perform FFT (real input signal)
583
# According to
https://haythamfayek.com/2016/04/21/speech-processing -for-machi
584
# NFFT is typically either 256 or 512
585
NFFT = 512
586
f_mag = np.abs(np.fft.rfft(frames, NFFT))
587
f_pow = (1.0 / NFFT) * (f_mag ** 2)
588
589
# Generate Mel filter banks
590
nfilt = 22
591
# Convert Hz to Mel (see
https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mel_scale)
592
low_freq_mel = 0
593
high_freq_mel = 2595 * np.log10(1 + (self.sr / 2) / 700)
594
# Equally spaced in Mel scale
595
mel_points = np.linspace(low_freq_mel , high_freq_mel ,
nfilt + 2)
596
hz_points = 700 * (10 ** (mel_points / 2595) - 1)
597
bin = np.floor((NFFT + 1) * hz_points / self.sr)
598
599
fbank = np.zeros((nfilt, int(np.floor(NFFT / 2 + 1))))
600
for m in range(1, nfilt + 1):
601
f_m_minus = int(bin[m - 1])
# left
602
f_m = int(bin[m])
# center
603
f_m_plus = int(bin[m + 1])
# right
604
==PAGE:128==
114
A Network Development
605
for k in range(f_m_minus , f_m):
606
fbank[m - 1, k] = (k - bin[m - 1]) / (bin[m] -
bin[m - 1])
607
for k in range(f_m, f_m_plus):
608
fbank[m - 1, k] = (bin[m + 1] - k) / (bin[m +
1] - bin[m])
609
610
filter_banks = np.dot(f_pow, fbank.T)
611
# Replace zeros with smallest positive number for
stability
612
filter_banks = np.where(filter_banks == 0,
np.finfo(float).eps, filter_banks)
613
# Convert to dB
614
filter_banks = 20 * np.log10(filter_banks)
615
# Fix negative dB numbers after normalization
616
filter_banks += np.abs(np.min(filter_banks))
617
# Filter out everything below the 80th percentile and
normalize
618
filter_banks = np.where(
619
filter_banks >= np.percentile(filter_banks , 80),
filter_banks , 0
620
)
621
filter_banks /= (np.max(filter_banks) + 1e-9)
622
623
# Append the signal and its label to the lists
624
with warnings.catch_warnings():
625
warnings.simplefilter("ignore")
626
audio.append(torch.Tensor(filter_banks[:nfilt,
:nfilt]))
627
labels.append(self.kws.index(label))
628
629
# Store result
630
self.audio, self.labels = torch.stack(audio),
torch.Tensor(labels)

class RateEncoder(Encoder):
==PAGE:129==
A.2 The kwsonsnn Library

8
def __init__(self, time: int, dt: float = 1.0, **kwargs):
9
"""
10
Creates a callable RateEncoder which encodes as defined in
``bindsnet.encoding ``
11
12
:param time: Length of Rate spike train per input variable.
13
:param dt: Simulation time step.
14
"""
15
super().__init__(time, dt=dt, **kwargs)
16
17
self.enc = rate
18
19
def rate(datum: torch.Tensor , time: int, dt: float = 1.0, **kwargs)
-> torch.Tensor:
20
"""
21
Direct rate based incoding
22
"""
23
assert (datum >= 0).all(), "Inputs must be non-negative"
24
25
# Get shape and size of data.
26
shape, size = datum.shape, datum.numel()
27
datum = datum.flatten()
28
time = int(time / dt)
29
30
# Compute firing rates in seconds as function of data intensity ,
31
# accounting for simulation time step.
32
rate = torch.zeros(size, device=datum.device)
33
rate[datum != 0] = 1 / datum[datum != 0] * (1000 / dt)
34
35
timeRates = torch.ones(time+1, size, device=datum.device)
36
for i in range(time+1):
37
timeRates[i] = rate
38
39
# Calculate spike times by cumulatively summing over time
dimension.
40
times = torch.cumsum(timeRates , dim=0).long()
41
times[times >= time + 1] = 0
42
43
# Create tensor of spikes.
44
spikes = torch.zeros(time + 1, size, device=datum.device).byte()
45
spikes[times, torch.arange(size)] = 1
46
spikes = spikes[1:]
47
48
return spikes.view(time, *shape)

def rankOrderPeriod(datum: torch.Tensor, time: int, dt: float =

96
"""
97

# Build a very simple model with two layers

54

def forward(self, X):
56
return F.softmax(self.ff2(F.relu(self.ff1(X.view(-1)))),
dim=0)

APPENDIX B
Accelerator Sources
This chapter includes all source files that, together, constitute the neuromorphic acceler-
ator. This includes all hardware description classes, the mapping script, the two Verilog
files that bind together the top-level design, and the constraints needed to synthesize
the accelerator in Vivado.
B.1
Chisel Source Files
The following are the source files constituting the neuromorphic accelerator. Files are
grouped according to their location in the accelerator architecture, and relative to the
hierarchy within each group.

==PAGE:167==

|/* verilator lint_off UNOPTFLAT */
49
|module ClockBufferBB(input I, input CE, output O);
50
|
reg en_latched /*verilator clock_enable*/;
51
|
always_latch @(*) if (!I) en_latched = CE;
52
|
assign O = en_latched & I;
53
|endmodule

}

Mapping Script
The following is the script to generate memory initialization files from a JSON file.
1
import neuroproc._
2
3
import java.io._
4
import spray.json._
5
6
/** Generates memory initialization files from a JSON file
7
*
8
* @param #1 the JSON file to read
9
*
10
* Run this script with SBT as
11
* {{{
12
* sbt "runMain MakeDataFiles path/to/json/file.json"
13
* }}}
14
* Output files are stored in ./mapping/meminit
==PAGE:207==
B.3 Mapping Script
193
15
*
16
* @note The encoding is custom and network specific. For now,
17
*
the following is used:
18
*
- Reset potential , refrac period , and decay are 5 bits
19
*
(converted on-the-fly)
20
*
- Potentials and refrac counts are 17 bits (7.10 and
21
*
17.0, respectively)
22
*
- Biases and thresholds are 17 bits (7.10)
23
*
- Weights are 11 bits (two different formats; learnt
24
*
weights 0.10, constant weights 6.4), MSB decides between
25
*
the two
26
*/
27
object MakeDataFiles extends App {
28
require(args.length > 0)
29
30
/** Returns a binary string representation of an integer
31
*
32
* @param x the integer to convert
33
* @param len the desired length of the string representation
34
* @return a binary string representation of `x` of length `len`
35
*/
36
def to2aryString(x: Int, len: Int) = {
37
val binStr = x.toBinaryString
38
if (binStr.length >= len) binStr.substring(binStr.length - len)
39
else s"${if (x >= 0) 0 else 1}" * (len - binStr.length) + binStr
40
}
41
42
// Fetch the parameters from the specified JSON file
43
val params = new ParameterReader(args(0))
44
45
// Create a directory for the files
46
val dir = new File("mapping/meminit")
47
dir.mkdir()
48
49
// Generate memory initialization files
50
for (core <- 2 until 4) {
51
// Constant memories
52
val cpw = new PrintWriter(new
File(s"mapping/meminit/constc${core}.mem"))
53
cpw.write(to2aryString(params.getMemData("reset", core)(0),
5)+"\n")
54
cpw.write(to2aryString(params.getMemData("refrac", core)(0),
5)+"\n")
55
cpw.write(to2aryString(params.getMemData("decay", core)(0),
5)+"\n")
56
cpw.write(to2aryString(0, 5)+"\n")
==PAGE:208==
194
B Accelerator Sources
57
cpw.close
58
59
// Potentials and refrac counters
60
val ppw = new PrintWriter(new
File(s"mapping/meminit/potrefc${core}.mem"))
61
ppw.write(Array.fill(2*TMNEURONS)(to2aryString(0,
17)).deep.mkString("\n")+"\n")
62
ppw.close
63
64
for (eval <- 0 until EVALUNITS) {
65
// Biases and thresholds
66
val bpw = new PrintWriter(
67
new File(s"mapping/meminit/biasthreshc${core}e${eval}.mem")
68
)
69
bpw.write(params.getMemData("biases", core, eval).map(
70
to2aryString(_, 17)).deep.mkString("\n")+"\n"
71
)
72
bpw.write(params.getMemData("thresh", core, eval).map(
73
to2aryString(_, 17)).deep.mkString("\n")+"\n"
74
)
75
bpw.close
76
77
// Weights
78
val wpw = new PrintWriter(
79
new File(s"mapping/meminit/weightsc${core}e${eval}.mem")
80
)
81
val wghts = params.getMemWeights(core, eval)
82
wpw.write(wghts.map { p =>
83
(if (p._1) "1" else "0") + to2aryString(p._2, 10)
84
}.deep.mkString("\n")+"\n")
85
wpw.close
86
}
87
}
88
}

/** Generates an array with memory data for a specified (coreID ,
evalID) pair
166
*
167
* @param name the field to look up
168
* @param coreID the neuron core ID of the memory
169
* @param evalID the neuron evaluator ID of the memory
170
* @param binPoint the number of fixed-point decimals to use for
171
*
Float parameters
172
* @return an Array[Int] with the initialization data for
173
*
parameter `name` for core `coreID ` evaluator `evalID `
174
*
with Float parameters mapped to fixed-point with
175
*
`binPoint ` decimal bits
176
*/
177
def getMemData(name: String , coreID: Int, evalID: Int = 0,
binPoint: Int = 10) = {
178
require(name != "w1" && name != "w2" && name != "w")
179
require(coreID == 2 || coreID == 3)
180
require(l1.contains(name) || l1F.contains(name) ||
l2.contains(name) || l2F.contains(name))
181
var memData = Array.fill(TMNEURONS)(0)
182
183
// Fetch the data from either map, which contains the given key
184
val intData = coreID match {
185
case 2 => if (l1.contains(name)) Some(l1(name)(0)) else None
186
case _ => if (l2.contains(name)) Some(l2(name)(0)) else None
187
}
188
val floatData = coreID match {
189
case 2 => if (l1F.contains(name)) Some(l1F(name)(0)) else None
==PAGE:211==
B.3 Mapping Script
197
190
case _ => if (l2F.contains(name)) Some(l2F(name)(0)) else None
191
}
192
193
// Fill out the memory data for the specified parameters
194
(intData , floatData) match {
195
case (Some(arr), None) =>
196
// Data only available for 200 neurons
197
for (i <- 0 until TMNEURONS)
198
if (i*EVALUNITS + evalID < 200)
199
memData(i) = arr(i*EVALUNITS + evalID)
200
case (None, Some(arr)) =>
201
// Data only available for 200 neurons
202
for (i <- 0 until TMNEURONS)
203
if (i*EVALUNITS + evalID < 200)
204
memData(i) = (arr(i*EVALUNITS + evalID) * (1 <<
binPoint)).round
205
case _ => throw new IllegalArgumentException("expects either
Int or Float data, but not neither nor both")
206
}
207
memData
208
}

/** Generates an array with weight memory data for a specified

def getMemWeights(coreID: Int, evalID: Int, binPoint1: Int = 10,

226

227

228

229

// Fill out the memory data for the specified parameters
231
coreID match {
==PAGE:212==
198
B Accelerator Sources
232
case 2 =>
233
for (i <- 0 until TMNEURONS) {
234

235
// Data only available for 200 neurons
236
if (idx < 200) {
237
for (j <- 0 until 3*256) {
238

239

240

(1 << binPoint1)).round)
241
// Weights for Ai_to_Ae connection
242

243

* (1 << binPoint2)).round)
244
}
245
}
246
}
247

248
for (i <- 0 until TMNEURONS) {
249

// Data only available for 200 neurons

if (idx < 200)

253

memData(i*3*256 + j) = (true, (allData1(j)(idx) * (1
<< binPoint2)).round)
256
}
257
}

memData
259

APPENDIX C
Hardware Testers
The following are all testing related source files. The files are split into unit tests, system
tests, and VHDL sources useful for testing in Vivado. Finally, the reference image and
expected spike results are also included here.
C.1
Unit Tests
The following are the source files for all unit tests implemented for single modules. The
files are organized following the same pattern as in appendix B.

Package File

/** Software model of the priority mask reset encoder
10
*
11
* @param reqs array of incoming requests
12
* @return tuple of index, mask, reset vector , and valid
13
*/
14
def priorityMask(reqs: Array[Boolean]) = {
15
require(reqs.length == EVALUNITS)
16
val valid = reqs.reduce(_ || _)
17
var value = 0
18
for (i <- EVALUNITS -1 to 0 by -1)
19
if (reqs(i))
20
value = i
21
var mask = Array(value == 0)
22
for (i <- EVALUNITS -2 to 0 by -1) {
23
mask = (reqs(i+1) || mask.head) +: mask
24
}
==PAGE:216==

25
val rst = (0 until EVALUNITS).map { _ == value && valid
}.toArray
26
(value, mask, rst, valid)
27
}

/** Tests the off-chip communication

= FREQ / SCALE

D.4
Flawed Chisel Top-level
The following is the Chisel top-level and MMCM blackbox declarations, which were
not used in the final version of the source code due to inexplicable higher resource
consumption.
1
import neuroproc._
2
3
import chisel3._
4
import chisel3.util.HasBlackBoxResource
